In view are the station\'s four large, gold-coloured solar array wings, two on either side of the station, mounted to a central truss structure. Further along the truss are six large, white radiators, three next to each pair of arrays. In between the solar arrays and radiators is a cluster of pressurised modules arranged in an elongated T shape, also attached to the truss. style="font-size:90%; line-height:15px;">Station elements as of June 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup><br />\n(<a href="/wiki/Exploded_view" class="mw-redirect" title="Exploded view">exploded view</a>)</div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr style="display:none">\n<td colspan="2"></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p>The <b>International Space Station</b> (<b>ISS</b>) is a <a href="/wiki/Space_station" title="Space station">space station</a>, or a habitable <a href="/wiki/Satellite" title="Satellite">artificial satellite</a>, in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>. Its first component launched into orbit in 1998, and the ISS is now the largest human-made body in low Earth orbit and can often be seen with the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a> from Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> The ISS consists of pressurised modules, external trusses, <a href="/wiki/Solar_arrays" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar arrays">solar arrays</a>, and other components. ISS components have been launched by Russian <a href="/wiki/Proton_(rocket_family)" title="Proton (rocket family)">Proton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(rocket_family)" title="Soyuz (rocket family)">Soyuz</a> rockets, and American <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSBook_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSBook-10">[10]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The ISS serves as a <a href="/wiki/Microgravity" class="mw-redirect" title="Microgravity">microgravity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_environment" title="Space environment">space environment</a> research laboratory in which crew members conduct experiments in <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_biology" title="Human biology">human biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">physics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Scientific_research_on_the_International_Space_Station" title="Scientific research on the International Space Station">other fields</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ISS_overview_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISS_overview-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA_Fields_of_Research_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA_ISS_Goals_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_ISS_Goals-13">[13]</a></sup> The station is suited for the testing of spacecraft systems and equipment required for missions to the Moon and Mars.<sup id="cite_ref-ResProg_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ResProg-14">[14]</a></sup> The ISS <a href="/wiki/Orbital_station-keeping" title="Orbital station-keeping">maintains an orbit with an altitude</a> of between 330 and 435&#160;km (205 and 270&#160;mi) by means of reboost manoeuvres using the engines of the <i><a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)">Zvezda</a></i> module or visiting spacecraft. It completes 15.54&#160;orbits per day.<sup id="cite_ref-tracking_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tracking-15">[15]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The ISS is the ninth space station to be inhabited by crews, following the Soviet and later Russian <i><a href="/wiki/Salyut_programme" title="Salyut programme">Salyut</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Almaz" title="Almaz">Almaz</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a></i> stations as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a></i> from the US. The station has been continuously occupied for <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7003620100000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>16&#160;years and 357&#160;days since the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Expedition_1" title="Expedition 1">Expedition 1</a> on 2 November 2000. This is the longest continuous human presence in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>, having surpassed the previous record of <span style="display:none" class="sortkey">7003364400000000000\xe2\x99\xa0</span>9&#160;years and 357&#160;days held by <i>Mir</i>. The station is serviced by a variety of visiting spacecraft: the Russian <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft)" title="Progress (spacecraft)">Progress</a>, the American <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon">Dragon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft)" title="Cygnus (spacecraft)">Cygnus</a>, the Japanese <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">H-II Transfer Vehicle</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup> and formerly the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> and the European <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">Automated Transfer Vehicle</a>. It has been visited by astronauts, cosmonauts and space tourists from <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_visitors" title="List of International Space Station visitors">17 different nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10th_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10th-17">[17]</a></sup></p>\n<p>After the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> programme ended in 2011, Soyuz rockets became the only provider of transport for astronauts at the International Space Station, and Dragon became the only provider of bulk cargo return to Earth (called <a href="/wiki/Downmass" class="mw-redirect" title="Downmass">downmass</a>). Soyuz has very limited downmass capability.</p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station_program" title="International Space Station program">ISS programme</a> is a joint project among five participating space agencies: <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Roscosmos_State_Corporation" class="mw-redirect" title="Roscosmos State Corporation">Roscosmos</a>, <a href="/wiki/JAXA" title="JAXA">JAXA</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">ESA</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Space_Agency" title="Canadian Space Agency">CSA</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PartStates_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PartStates-18">[18]</a></sup> The ownership and use of the space station is established by intergovernmental treaties and agreements.<sup id="cite_ref-ESA-IGA_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-IGA-19">[19]</a></sup> The station is divided into two sections, the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment" title="Russian Orbital Segment">Russian Orbital Segment</a> (ROS) and the <a href="/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment" title="US Orbital Segment">United States Orbital Segment</a> (USOS), which is shared by many nations. As of January 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the American portion of ISS is being funded until 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Roscosmos has endorsed the continued operation of ISS through 2024<sup id="cite_ref-sn20150225_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sn20150225-23">[23]</a></sup> but has proposed using elements of the Russian Orbital Segment to construct a new Russian space station called <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Piloted_Assembly_and_Experiment_Complex" title="Orbital Piloted Assembly and Experiment Complex">OPSEK</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-moscow20141117_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-moscow20141117-24">[24]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On 28 March 2015, Russian sources announced that Roscosmos and NASA had agreed to collaborate on the development of a replacement for the current ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-independent20150328_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent20150328-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rtcom20150328_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rtcom20150328-26">[26]</a></sup> NASA later issued a guarded statement expressing thanks for Russia\'s interest in future co-operation in space exploration but fell short of confirming the Russian announcement.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-no_plans_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-no_plans-28">[28]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="toclimit-2">\n<div id="toc" class="toc">\n<div class="toctitle">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a class="toctext">Multimedia</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Purpose">Purpose</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" data-file-height="2848" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-134_EVA4_view_to_the_Space_Shuttle_Endeavour.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nFisheye view of several labs</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg/220px-NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg/440px-NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NanoRacksCubeSatLaunch_ISS038-E-056389.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<a href="/wiki/CubeSat" title="CubeSat">CubeSats</a> are deployed by the <a href="/wiki/NanoRacks_CubeSat_Deployer" title="NanoRacks CubeSat Deployer">NanoRacks CubeSat Deployer</a> attached to the end of the Japanese robotic arm</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>According to the original Memorandum of Understanding between NASA and Rosaviakosmos, the International Space Station was intended to be a laboratory, observatory and factory in <a href="/wiki/Low_Earth_orbit" title="Low Earth orbit">low Earth orbit</a>. It was also planned to provide transportation, maintenance, and act as a staging base for possible future missions to the Moon, Mars and asteroids.<sup id="cite_ref-RSA-MOU_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RSA-MOU-29">[29]</a></sup> In the 2010 United States National Space Policy, the ISS was given additional roles of serving commercial, diplomatic<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and educational purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-USNSP_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-USNSP-31">[31]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scientific_research">Scientific research</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Scientific_research_on_the_ISS" class="mw-redirect" title="Scientific research on the ISS">Scientific research on the ISS</a></div>\n<p>The ISS provides a platform to conduct scientific research. Small unmanned spacecraft can provide platforms for zero gravity and exposure to space, but space stations offer a long-term environment where studies can be performed potentially for decades, combined with ready access by human researchers over periods that exceed the capabilities of manned spacecraft.<sup id="cite_ref-10th_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10th-17">[17]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Worldbook_at_NASA_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldbook_at_NASA-32">[32]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The ISS simplifies individual experiments by eliminating the need for separate rocket launches and research staff. The wide variety of research fields include <a href="/wiki/Astrobiology" title="Astrobiology">astrobiology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zero_g#Health_effects_of_weightlessness" class="mw-redirect" title="Zero g">human research</a> including <a href="/wiki/Space_medicine" title="Space medicine">space medicine</a> and <a href="/wiki/Life_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Life science">life sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physical_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Physical science">physical sciences</a>, <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Space_weather" title="Space weather">space weather</a>, and weather on Earth (<a href="/wiki/Meteorology" title="Meteorology">meteorology</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-ISS_overview_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISS_overview-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA_Fields_of_Research_12-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA_ISS_Goals_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_ISS_Goals-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Scientists on Earth have access to the crew\'s data and can modify experiments or launch new ones, which are benefits generally unavailable on unmanned spacecraft.<sup id="cite_ref-Worldbook_at_NASA_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldbook_at_NASA-32">[32]</a></sup> Crews fly <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_expeditions" title="List of International Space Station expeditions">expeditions</a> of several months duration, providing approximately 160-man-hours per week of labour with a crew of 6.<sup id="cite_ref-ISS_overview_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISS_overview-11">[11]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Science_in_School_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Science_in_School-35">[35]</a></sup></p>\n<p>To detect dark matter and answer other fundamental questions about our universe, engineers and scientists from all over the world built the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer" title="Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer">Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer</a> (AMS), which NASA compares to the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_space_telescope" class="mw-redirect" title="Hubble space telescope">Hubble space telescope</a>, and says could not be accommodated on a free flying satellite platform partly because of its power requirements and data bandwidth needs.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> On 3 April 2013, <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a> scientists reported that hints of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> may have been detected by the Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer.<sup id="cite_ref-APS-20130403_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-APS-20130403-38">[38]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AMS-20130403_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMS-20130403-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-AP-20130403_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP-20130403-40">[40]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC-20130403_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20130403-41">[41]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASA-20130403_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-20130403-42">[42]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20130403_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20130403-43">[43]</a></sup> According to the scientists, "<a href="/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer#First_results" title="Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer">The first results</a> from the space-borne Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer confirm an unexplained excess of high-energy positrons in Earth-bound cosmic rays."</p>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:366px;max-width:366px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:119px;max-width:119px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg/117px-Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg" width="117" height="159" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg/176px-Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg/234px-Iss030e015472_Edit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2832" data-file-height="3857" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/C/2011_W3_(Lovejoy)" title="C/2011 W3 (Lovejoy)">Comet Lovejoy</a> photographed by <a href="/wiki/Expedition_30" title="Expedition 30">Expedition 30</a> commander <a href="/wiki/Dan_Burbank" class="mw-redirect" title="Dan Burbank">Dan Burbank</a></div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:243px;max-width:243px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg/241px-ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg" width="241" height="159" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg/362px-ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg/482px-ISS-08_Michael_Foale_conducts_an_inspection_of_the_Microgravity_Science_Glovebox.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2002" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Expedition_8" title="Expedition 8">Expedition 8</a> Commander and Science Officer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Foale" title="Michael Foale">Michael Foale</a> conducts an inspection of the <a href="/wiki/Microgravity_Science_Glovebox" title="Microgravity Science Glovebox">Microgravity Science Glovebox</a></div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The space environment is hostile to life. Unprotected presence in space is characterised by an intense radiation field (consisting primarily of protons and other subatomic charged particles from the <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a>, in addition to <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>), high vacuum, extreme temperatures, and microgravity.<sup id="cite_ref-Space_Microbiology_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Space_Microbiology-44">[44]</a></sup> Some simple forms of life called <a href="/wiki/Extremophile" title="Extremophile">extremophiles</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Beer_microbes_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beer_microbes-45">[45]</a></sup> including small invertebrates called <a href="/wiki/Tardigrade" title="Tardigrade">tardigrades</a><sup id="cite_ref-Waterbears_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Waterbears-46">[46]</a></sup> can survive in this environment in an extremely dry state called <a href="/wiki/Desiccation#Biology_and_ecology" title="Desiccation">desiccation</a>.</p>\n<p>Medical research improves knowledge about the effects of long-term space exposure on the human body, including <a href="/wiki/Muscle_atrophy" title="Muscle atrophy">muscle atrophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Osteoporosis" title="Osteoporosis">bone loss</a>, and fluid shift. This data will be used to determine whether lengthy <a href="/wiki/Human_spaceflight" title="Human spaceflight">human spaceflight</a> and <a href="/wiki/Space_colonisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Space colonisation">space colonisation</a> are feasible. As of 2006<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, data on bone loss and muscular atrophy suggest that there would be a significant risk of fractures and movement problems if astronauts landed on a planet after a lengthy interplanetary cruise, such as the six-month interval required to <a href="/wiki/Manned_mission_to_Mars" class="mw-redirect" title="Manned mission to Mars">travel to Mars</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JCB_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCB-47">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> Medical studies are conducted aboard the ISS on behalf of the <a href="/wiki/National_Space_Biomedical_Research_Institute" title="National Space Biomedical Research Institute">National Space Biomedical Research Institute</a> (NSBRI). Prominent among these is the <a href="/wiki/Advanced_Diagnostic_Ultrasound_in_Microgravity" title="Advanced Diagnostic Ultrasound in Microgravity">Advanced Diagnostic Ultrasound in Microgravity</a> study in which astronauts perform ultrasound scans under the guidance of remote experts. The study considers the diagnosis and treatment of medical conditions in space. Usually, there is no physician on board the ISS and diagnosis of medical conditions is a challenge. It is anticipated that remotely guided ultrasound scans will have application on Earth in emergency and rural care situations where access to a trained physician is difficult.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Microgravity">Microgravity</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Space_Fire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Space_Fire.jpg/220px-Space_Fire.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Space_Fire.jpg/330px-Space_Fire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Space_Fire.jpg/440px-Space_Fire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="771" data-file-height="600" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Space_Fire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA comparison between the combustion of a candle on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> (left) and in a microgravity environment, such as that found on the ISS (right)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The Earth\'s gravity is only slightly weaker at the altitude of the ISS than at the surface, but objects in orbit are in a continuous state of <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">freefall</a>, resulting in an apparent state of weightlessness. This perceived weightlessness is disturbed by five separate effects:<sup id="cite_ref-gravity_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gravity-52">[52]</a></sup></p>\n<ul>\n<li>Drag from the residual atmosphere; when the ISS enters the Earth\'s shadow, the main solar panels are rotated to minimise this aerodynamic drag, helping reduce <a href="/wiki/Orbital_decay" title="Orbital decay">orbital decay</a>.</li>\n<li>Vibration from movements of mechanical systems and the crew.</li>\n<li>Actuation of the on-board attitude <a href="/wiki/Control_moment_gyroscope" title="Control moment gyroscope">control moment gyroscopes</a>.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Rocket_engine" title="Rocket engine">Thruster</a> firings for attitude or orbital changes.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gravity-gradient_stabilization" title="Gravity-gradient stabilization">Gravity-gradient effects</a>, also known as <a href="/wiki/Tidal_force" title="Tidal force">tidal</a> effects. Items at different locations within the ISS would, if not attached to the station, follow slightly different orbits. Being mechanically interconnected these items experience small forces that keep the station moving as a <a href="/wiki/Rigid_body" title="Rigid body">rigid body</a>.</li>\n</ul>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg/220px-ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg/330px-ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg/440px-ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2040" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS-20_Robert_Thirsk_at_the_Minus_Eighty_Degree_Laboratory_Freezer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nISS crew member storing samples</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Researchers are investigating the effect of the station\'s near-weightless environment on the evolution, development, growth and internal processes of plants and animals. In response to some of this data, NASA wants to investigate <a href="/wiki/Microgravity" class="mw-redirect" title="Microgravity">microgravity</a>\'s effects on the growth of three-dimensional, human-like tissues, and the unusual <a href="/wiki/Protein_crystal" class="mw-redirect" title="Protein crystal">protein crystals</a> that can be formed in space.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_Fields_of_Research_12-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Investigating the physics of fluids in microgravity will provide better models of the behaviour of fluids. Because fluids can be almost completely combined in microgravity, physicists investigate fluids that do not mix well on Earth. In addition, examining reactions that are slowed by low gravity and low temperatures will improve our understanding of <a href="/wiki/Superconductivity" title="Superconductivity">superconductivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_Fields_of_Research_12-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The study of <a href="/wiki/Materials_science" title="Materials science">materials science</a> is an important ISS research activity, with the objective of reaping economic benefits through the improvement of techniques used on the ground.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> Other areas of interest include the effect of the low gravity environment on combustion, through the study of the efficiency of burning and control of emissions and pollutants. These findings may improve current knowledge about energy production, and lead to economic and environmental benefits. Future plans are for the researchers aboard the ISS to examine <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_vapour" class="mw-redirect" title="Water vapour">water vapour</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oxide" title="Oxide">oxides</a> in Earth\'s atmosphere, as well as <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_dust" title="Cosmic dust">cosmic dust</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> in the universe.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_Fields_of_Research_12-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_Fields_of_Research-12">[12]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mars500.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Mars500.jpg/220px-Mars500.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Mars500.jpg/330px-Mars500.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Mars500.jpg/440px-Mars500.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="555" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mars500.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA 3D plan of the Russia-based <a href="/wiki/MARS-500" title="MARS-500">MARS-500</a> complex, used for ground-based experiments which complement ISS-based preparations for a <a href="/wiki/Human_mission_to_Mars" title="Human mission to Mars">human mission to Mars</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The ISS provides a location in the relative safety of Low Earth Orbit to test spacecraft systems that will be required for long-duration missions to <a href="/wiki/The_Moon" class="mw-redirect" title="The Moon">the Moon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>. This provides experience in operations, maintenance as well as repair and replacement activities on-orbit, which will be essential skills in operating spacecraft farther from Earth, mission risks can be reduced and the capabilities of interplanetary spacecraft advanced.<sup id="cite_ref-ResProg_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ResProg-14">[14]</a></sup> Referring to the <a href="/wiki/MARS-500" title="MARS-500">MARS-500</a> experiment, ESA states that "Whereas the ISS is essential for answering questions concerning the possible impact of weightlessness, radiation and other space-specific factors, aspects such as the effect of long-term isolation and confinement can be more appropriately addressed via ground-based simulations".<sup id="cite_ref-esa_mars500_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-esa_mars500-54">[54]</a></sup> Sergey Krasnov, the head of human space flight programmes for Russia\'s space agency, Roscosmos, in 2011 suggested a "shorter version" of MARS-500 may be carried out on the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-Mars_thing_on_ISS_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_thing_on_ISS-55">[55]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2009, noting the value of the partnership framework itself, Sergey Krasnov wrote, "When compared with partners acting separately, partners developing complementary abilities and resources could give us much more assurance of the success and safety of space exploration. The ISS is helping further advance near-Earth space exploration and realisation of prospective programmes of research and exploration of the Solar system, including the Moon and Mars."<sup id="cite_ref-IAF2009_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IAF2009-56">[56]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manned_mission_to_Mars" class="mw-redirect" title="Manned mission to Mars">A manned mission to Mars</a> may be a multinational effort involving space agencies and countries outside the current ISS partnership. In 2010, ESA Director-General Jean-Jacques Dordain stated his agency was ready to propose to the other four partners that China, India and South Korea be invited to join the ISS partnership.<sup id="cite_ref-ESAproposesInvite_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESAproposesInvite-57">[57]</a></sup> NASA chief <a href="/wiki/Charlie_Bolden" class="mw-redirect" title="Charlie Bolden">Charlie Bolden</a> stated in February 2011, "Any mission to Mars is likely to be a global effort".<sup id="cite_ref-Mars_a_global_effort_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mars_a_global_effort-58">[58]</a></sup> Currently, American legislation prevents NASA co-operation with China on space projects.<sup id="cite_ref-justice1_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-justice1-59">[59]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education_and_cultural_outreach">Education and cultural outreach</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg/220px-ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg/330px-ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg/440px-ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1936" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS-36_HTV-4_berthing_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nJapan\'s Kounotori 4 berthing</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The ISS crew provides opportunities for students on Earth by running student-developed experiments, making educational demonstrations, allowing for student participation in classroom versions of ISS experiments, and directly engaging students using radio, videolink and email.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> ESA offers a wide range of free teaching materials that can be downloaded for use in classrooms.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> In one lesson, students can navigate a 3-D model of the interior and exterior of the ISS, and face spontaneous challenges to solve in real time.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>\n<p>JAXA aims both to "Stimulate the curiosity of children, cultivating their spirits, and encouraging their passion to pursue craftsmanship", and to "Heighten the child\'s awareness of the importance of life and their responsibilities in society."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> Through a series of education guides, a deeper understanding of the past and near-term future of manned space flight, as well as that of Earth and life, will be learned.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> In the JAXA Seeds in Space experiments, the mutation effects of spaceflight on plant seeds aboard the ISS is explored. Students grow sunflower seeds which flew on the ISS for about nine months as a start to \'touch the Universe\'. In the first phase of <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> utilisation from 2008 to mid-2010, researchers from more than a dozen Japanese universities conducted experiments in diverse fields.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg/220px-Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg/330px-Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg/440px-Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2040" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Crew_in_ATV_with_Jules_Verne_manuscript.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nOriginal Jules Verne manuscripts displayed by crew inside Jules Verne ATV</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Cultural activities are another major objective. Tetsuo Tanaka, director of JAXA\'s Space Environment and Utilization Center, says "There is something about space that touches even people who are not interested in science."<sup id="cite_ref-jaxa2_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jaxa2-67">[67]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/ARISS" class="mw-redirect" title="ARISS">Amateur Radio on the ISS</a> (ARISS) is a volunteer programme which encourages students worldwide to pursue careers in science, technology, engineering and mathematics through <a href="/wiki/Amateur_radio" title="Amateur radio">amateur radio</a> communications opportunities with the ISS crew. ARISS is an international working group, consisting of delegations from nine countries including several countries in Europe as well as Japan, Russia, Canada, and the United States. In areas where radio equipment cannot be used, speakerphones connect students to ground stations which then connect the calls to the station.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>\n<p><i><a href="/wiki/First_Orbit" title="First Orbit">First Orbit</a></i> is a feature-length documentary film about <a href="/wiki/Vostok_1" title="Vostok 1">Vostok 1</a>, the first manned space flight around the Earth. By matching the orbit of the International Space Station to that of Vostok 1 as closely as possible, in terms of ground path and time of day, documentary filmmaker Christopher Riley and ESA astronaut <a href="/wiki/Paolo_Nespoli" title="Paolo Nespoli">Paolo Nespoli</a> were able to film the view that <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Gagarin" title="Yuri Gagarin">Yuri Gagarin</a> saw on his pioneering orbital space flight. This new footage was cut together with the original Vostok 1 mission audio recordings sourced from the Russian State Archive. Nespoli, during Expedition 26/27, filmed the majority of the footage for this documentary film, and as a result is credited as its <a href="/wiki/Cinematographer" title="Cinematographer">director of photography</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The film was streamed through the website firstorbit.org in a global YouTube premiere in 2011, under a free license.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In May 2013, commander <a href="/wiki/Chris_Hadfield" title="Chris Hadfield">Chris Hadfield</a> shot a music video of <a href="/wiki/David_Bowie" title="David Bowie">David Bowie</a>\'s "<a href="/wiki/Space_Oddity" title="Space Oddity">Space Oddity</a>" on board the station; the film was released freely on YouTube.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> It was the first music video ever to be filmed in space.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Assembly">Assembly</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Assembly_of_the_International_Space_Station" title="Assembly of the International Space Station">Assembly of the International Space Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_ISS_spacewalks" class="mw-redirect" title="List of ISS spacewalks">List of ISS spacewalks</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:International_Space_Station.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/International_Space_Station.jpg/220px-International_Space_Station.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/International_Space_Station.jpg/330px-International_Space_Station.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/International_Space_Station.jpg/440px-International_Space_Station.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2906" data-file-height="1924" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:International_Space_Station.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nISS in 2002, with P6-S0 truss</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_after_STS-116_in_December_2006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/ISS_after_STS-116_in_December_2006.jpg/220px-ISS_after_STS-116_in_December_2006.jpg" width="220" height="153" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/ISS_after_STS-116_in_December_2006.jpg/330px-ISS_after_STS-116_in_December_2006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/ISS_after_STS-116_in_December_2006.jpg/440px-ISS_after_STS-116_in_December_2006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2816" data-file-height="1952" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_after_STS-116_in_December_2006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nISS in 2006, with P3/P4 truss added</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zvezda_rear.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Zvezda_rear.jpg/220px-Zvezda_rear.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Zvezda_rear.jpg/330px-Zvezda_rear.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Zvezda_rear.jpg/440px-Zvezda_rear.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2968" data-file-height="1984" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zvezda_rear.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nAft view showing a Progress spacecraft docked to <i>Zvezda</i></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_after_STS-118_in_August_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ISS_after_STS-118_in_August_2007.jpg/220px-ISS_after_STS-118_in_August_2007.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ISS_after_STS-118_in_August_2007.jpg/330px-ISS_after_STS-118_in_August_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/ISS_after_STS-118_in_August_2007.jpg/440px-ISS_after_STS-118_in_August_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2928" data-file-height="1872" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_after_STS-118_in_August_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nISS in 2007, with S3/S4 truss added</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_March_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/ISS_March_2009.jpg/220px-ISS_March_2009.jpg" width="220" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/ISS_March_2009.jpg/330px-ISS_March_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/ISS_March_2009.jpg/440px-ISS_March_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2464" data-file-height="1856" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_March_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nISS in 2009, with all trusses and solar arrays installed</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exterior_of_Cupola_-_Exp28.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Exterior_of_Cupola_-_Exp28.jpg/220px-Exterior_of_Cupola_-_Exp28.jpg" width="220" height="164" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Exterior_of_Cupola_-_Exp28.jpg/330px-Exterior_of_Cupola_-_Exp28.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Exterior_of_Cupola_-_Exp28.jpg/440px-Exterior_of_Cupola_-_Exp28.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3424" data-file-height="2560" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exterior_of_Cupola_-_Exp28.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nCupola arrived in 2010</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The assembly of the International Space Station, a major endeavour in <a href="/wiki/Space_architecture" title="Space architecture">space architecture</a>, began in November 1998.<sup id="cite_ref-OnOrbit_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OnOrbit-5">[5]</a></sup> Russian modules launched and docked robotically, with the exception of <i><a href="/wiki/Rassvet_(ISS_module)" title="Rassvet (ISS module)">Rassvet</a></i>. All other modules were delivered by the Space Shuttle, which required installation by ISS and shuttle crewmembers using the <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Servicing_System#Canadarm2" title="Mobile Servicing System">Canadarm2</a> (SSRMS) and <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_activity" title="Extravehicular activity">extra-vehicular activities</a> (EVAs); as of 5 June 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, they had added 159 components during more than 1,000 hours of EVA (see <a href="/wiki/List_of_ISS_spacewalks" class="mw-redirect" title="List of ISS spacewalks">List of ISS spacewalks</a>). 127 of these spacewalks originated from the station, and the remaining 32 were launched from the airlocks of docked Space Shuttles.<sup id="cite_ref-ISStD_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISStD-4">[4]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Beta_angle" title="Beta angle">beta angle</a> of the station had to be considered at all times during construction, as it directly affects how long during its orbit the station (and any docked or docking spacecraft) is exposed to the sun; the Space Shuttle would not perform optimally above a limit called the "beta cutoff".<sup id="cite_ref-mcc_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mcc-73">[73]</a></sup> Many of the modules that launched on the Space Shuttle were <a href="/wiki/Space_Station_Processing_Facility#Station_Integration_Testing" title="Space Station Processing Facility">integrated and tested on the ground</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Space_Station_Processing_Facility" title="Space Station Processing Facility">Space Station Processing Facility</a> to find and correct issues prior to launch.</p>\n<p>The first module of the ISS, <i><a href="/wiki/Zarya" title="Zarya">Zarya</a></i>, was launched on 20 November 1998 on an autonomous Russian <a href="/wiki/Proton_(rocket)" class="mw-redirect" title="Proton (rocket)">Proton rocket</a>. It provided propulsion, <a href="/wiki/Attitude_control" title="Attitude control">attitude control</a>, communications, electrical power, but lacked long-term life support functions. Two weeks later, a passive NASA module <i><a href="/wiki/Unity_(ISS_module)" title="Unity (ISS module)">Unity</a></i> was launched aboard Space Shuttle flight <a href="/wiki/STS-88" title="STS-88">STS-88</a> and attached to <i>Zarya</i> by astronauts during EVAs. This module has two <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_Mating_Adapter" title="Pressurized Mating Adapter">Pressurized Mating Adapters</a> (PMAs), one connects permanently to <i>Zarya</i>, the other allows the Space Shuttle to dock to the space station. At that time, the Russian station <i>Mir</i> was still inhabited. The ISS remained unmanned for two years, while <i>Mir</i> was de-orbited. On 12 July 2000, <i><a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)">Zvezda</a></i> was launched into orbit. Preprogrammed commands on board deployed its solar arrays and communications antenna. It then became the passive target for a rendezvous with <i>Zarya</i> and <i>Unity</i>: it maintained a station-keeping orbit while the <i>Zarya</i>-<i>Unity</i> vehicle performed the rendezvous and docking via ground control and the Russian automated rendezvous and docking system. <i>Zarya</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> computer transferred control of the station to <i>Zvezda</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> computer soon after docking. <i>Zvezda</i> added sleeping quarters, a toilet, kitchen, CO<sub>2</sub> scrubbers, dehumidifier, oxygen generators, exercise equipment, plus data, voice and television communications with mission control. This enabled permanent habitation of the station.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The first resident crew, <a href="/wiki/Expedition_1" title="Expedition 1">Expedition 1</a>, arrived in November 2000 on <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_TM-31" title="Soyuz TM-31">Soyuz TM-31</a>. At the end of the first day on the station, astronaut Bill Shepherd requested the use of the radio call sign "<i>Alpha</i>", which he and cosmonaut Krikalev preferred to the more cumbersome "<i>International Space Station</i>".<sup id="cite_ref-TIME-Nov2_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TIME-Nov2-76">[76]</a></sup> The name "<i>Alpha</i>" had previously been used for the station in the early 1990s,<sup id="cite_ref-GAO_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GAO-77">[77]</a></sup> and following the request, its use was authorised for the whole of Expedition 1.<sup id="cite_ref-SPACE-Nov3_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPACE-Nov3-78">[78]</a></sup> Shepherd had been advocating the use of a new name to project managers for some time. Referencing a <a href="/wiki/Ship_naming_and_launching" class="mw-redirect" title="Ship naming and launching">naval tradition</a> in a pre-launch news conference he had said: "For thousands of years, humans have been going to sea in ships. People have designed and built these vessels, launched them with a good feeling that a name will bring good fortune to the crew and success to their voyage."<sup id="cite_ref-SPACE-Nov2_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPACE-Nov2-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Semenov" title="Yuri Semenov">Yuri Semenov</a>, the President of <a href="/wiki/S.P._Korolev_Rocket_and_Space_Corporation_Energia" class="mw-redirect" title="S.P. Korolev Rocket and Space Corporation Energia">Russian Space Corporation Energia</a> at the time, disapproved of the name "<i>Alpha</i>"; he felt that <i>Mir</i> was the first space station, and so he would have preferred the names "<i>Beta</i>" or "<i>Mir</i>&#160;2" for the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-SPACE-Nov3_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPACE-Nov3-78">[78]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Expedition_1" title="Expedition 1">Expedition 1</a> arrived midway between the flights of <a href="/wiki/STS-92" title="STS-92">STS-92</a> and <a href="/wiki/STS-97" title="STS-97">STS-97</a>. These two Space Shuttle flights each added segments of the station\'s <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure" title="Integrated Truss Structure">Integrated Truss Structure</a>, which provided the station with Ku-band communication for US television, additional attitude support needed for the additional mass of the USOS, and substantial <a href="/wiki/Solar_array" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar array">solar arrays</a> supplementing the station\'s existing 4 solar arrays.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Over the next two years, the station continued to expand. A <a href="/wiki/Soyuz-U" title="Soyuz-U">Soyuz-U</a> rocket delivered the <a href="/wiki/Pirs_(ISS_module)" title="Pirs (ISS module)"><i>Pirs</i> docking compartment</a>. The Space Shuttles <i>Discovery</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis">Atlantis</a></i>, and <i>Endeavour</i> delivered the <a href="/wiki/Destiny_(ISS_module)" title="Destiny (ISS module)"><i>Destiny</i> laboratory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Quest_Joint_Airlock" title="Quest Joint Airlock"><i>Quest</i> airlock</a>, in addition to the station\'s main robot arm, the <i><a href="/wiki/Canadarm2" class="mw-redirect" title="Canadarm2">Canadarm2</a></i>, and several more segments of the Integrated Truss Structure.</p>\n<p>The expansion schedule was interrupted by the <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia" title="Space Shuttle Columbia"><i>Columbia</i></a> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster">disaster</a> in 2003 and a resulting two-year hiatus in the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_program" title="Space Shuttle program">Space Shuttle programme</a>. The space shuttle was grounded until 2005 with <a href="/wiki/STS-114" title="STS-114">STS-114</a> flown by <i>Discovery</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Assembly resumed in 2006 with the arrival of <a href="/wiki/STS-115" title="STS-115">STS-115</a> with <i>Atlantis</i>, which delivered the station\'s second set of solar arrays. Several more truss segments and a third set of arrays were delivered on <a href="/wiki/STS-116" title="STS-116">STS-116</a>, <a href="/wiki/STS-117" title="STS-117">STS-117</a>, and <a href="/wiki/STS-118" title="STS-118">STS-118</a>. As a result of the major expansion of the station\'s power-generating capabilities, more pressurised modules could be accommodated, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Harmony_(ISS_module)" title="Harmony (ISS module)">Harmony</a></i> node and <i>Columbus</i> European laboratory were added. These were soon followed by the first two components of <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i>. In March 2009, <a href="/wiki/STS-119" title="STS-119">STS-119</a> completed the Integrated Truss Structure with the installation of the fourth and final set of solar arrays. The final section of <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> was delivered in July 2009 on <a href="/wiki/STS-127" title="STS-127">STS-127</a>, followed by the Russian <i><a href="/wiki/Poisk_(ISS_module)" title="Poisk (ISS module)">Poisk</a></i> module. The third node, <i><a href="/wiki/Tranquility_(ISS_module)" title="Tranquility (ISS module)">Tranquility</a></i>, was delivered in February 2010 during <a href="/wiki/STS-130" title="STS-130">STS-130</a> by the Space Shuttle <i>Endeavour</i>, alongside the <a href="/wiki/Cupola_(ISS_module)" title="Cupola (ISS module)">Cupola</a>, followed in May 2010 by the penultimate Russian module, <i><a href="/wiki/Rassvet_(ISS_module)" title="Rassvet (ISS module)">Rassvet</a></i>. <i>Rassvet</i> was delivered by Space Shuttle <i>Atlantis</i> on <a href="/wiki/STS-132" title="STS-132">STS-132</a> in exchange for the Russian Proton delivery of the <i>Zarya</i> module in 1998 which had been funded by the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The last pressurised module of the USOS, <i>Leonardo</i>, was brought to the station by <i>Discovery</i> on her final flight, <a href="/wiki/STS-133" title="STS-133">STS-133</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> followed by the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer" title="Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer">Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer</a> on <a href="/wiki/STS-134" title="STS-134">STS-134</a>, delivered by <i>Endeavour</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>\n<p>As of June 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the station consisted of 15 pressurised modules and the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure" title="Integrated Truss Structure">Integrated Truss Structure</a>. Five modules are still to be launched, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Nauka_(ISS_module)" title="Nauka (ISS module)">Nauka</a></i> with the <a href="/wiki/European_Robotic_Arm" title="European Robotic Arm">European Robotic Arm</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Uzlovoy_Module" title="Uzlovoy Module">Uzlovoy Module</a>, and two power modules called <a href="/wiki/Science-Power_Module-1" title="Science-Power Module-1">NEM-1</a> and NEM-2.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> As of August 2017<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Russia\'s future primary research module <i>Nauka</i> is set to launch in the first quarter of 2018, along with the European Robotic Arm which will be able to relocate itself to different parts of the Russian modules of the station. After the <i>Nauka</i> module is attached, the Uzlovoy Module will be attached to one of its docking ports. When completed, the station will have a mass of more than 400 tonnes (440 short tons).<sup id="cite_ref-OnOrbit_5-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OnOrbit-5">[5]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The gross mass of the station changes over time. The total launch mass of the modules on orbit is about 417,289&#160;kg (919,965&#160;lb) (as of 3 September 2011).<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The mass of experiments, spare parts, personal effects, crew, foodstuff, clothing, propellants, water supplies, gas supplies, docked spacecraft, and other items add to the total mass of the station. Hydrogen gas is constantly vented overboard by the oxygen generators.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Station_structure">Station structure</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:310px;max-width:310px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:144px;max-width:144px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:ROS_Windows_0114_complete.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/ROS_Windows_0114_complete.jpg/142px-ROS_Windows_0114_complete.jpg" width="142" height="107" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/ROS_Windows_0114_complete.jpg/213px-ROS_Windows_0114_complete.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/ROS_Windows_0114_complete.jpg/284px-ROS_Windows_0114_complete.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1096" data-file-height="823" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Russian Orbital Segment Windows</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:USOS_window_identification.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/USOS_window_identification.png/160px-USOS_window_identification.png" width="160" height="106" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/USOS_window_identification.png/240px-USOS_window_identification.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/USOS_window_identification.png/320px-USOS_window_identification.png 2x" data-file-width="1043" data-file-height="688" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">USOS International Space Station window locations</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The ISS is a third generation<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> modular space station.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> Modular stations can allow the mission to be changed over time and new modules can be added or removed from the existing structure, allowing greater flexibility.</p>\n<p>Below is a diagram of major station components. The blue areas are pressurised sections accessible by the crew without using spacesuits. The station\'s unpressurised superstructure is indicated in red. Other unpressurised components are yellow. Note that the <i>Unity</i> node joins directly to the <i>Destiny</i> laboratory. For clarity, they are shown apart.</p>\n<table style="border-spacing: 0px; font-size:78%;line-height:100%;line-width:100%;padding:15px;border:2px dotted #AAA;">\n<tr style="height:1px;text-align:center">\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2" style="height:2em;width:2em"></td>\n<td colspan="2" rowspan="2"
