style="height:1px;text-align:center">\n<td></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comparison">Comparison</span></h3>\n<p>The ISS follows <i><a href="/wiki/Salyut" class="mw-redirect" title="Salyut">Salyut</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Almaz" title="Almaz">Almaz</a></i> series, <i><a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a></i> as the 11th space station launched, as the <i><a href="/wiki/Genesis_II" title="Genesis II">Genesis</a></i> prototypes were never intended to be manned. Other examples of modular station projects include the Soviet/Russian <i>Mir</i> and the planned Russian <a href="/wiki/OPSEK" class="mw-redirect" title="OPSEK">OPSEK</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_space_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Chinese space station">Chinese space station</a>. First generation space stations, such as early <i>Salyuts</i> and NASA\'s <i>Skylab</i> were not designed for re-supply.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Generally, each crew had to depart the station to free the only docking port for the next crew to arrive, <i>Skylab</i> had more than one docking port but was not designed for resupply. <a href="/wiki/Salyut_6" title="Salyut 6"><i>Salyut</i> 6</a> and <a href="/wiki/Salyut_7" title="Salyut 7">7</a> had more than one docking port and were designed to be resupplied routinely during crewed operation.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pressurised_modules">Pressurised modules</span></h2>\n<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation">\n<tr>\n<td class="mbox-image">\n<div style="width:52px"><a href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" width="50" height="39" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="399" /></a></div>\n</td>\n<td class="mbox-text">\n<div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small><i>(November 2015)</i></small> <small class="hide-when-compact"><i>(<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zarya"><i>Zarya</i></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg/170px-Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg" width="170" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg/255px-Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg/340px-Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1792" data-file-height="1984" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zarya_from_STS-88.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i>Zarya</i> as seen by <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour">Space Shuttle Endeavour</a> during <a href="/wiki/STS-88" title="STS-88">STS-88</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Zarya" title="Zarya">Zarya</a></b></i> (Russian: \xd0\x97\xd0\xb0\xd1\x80\xd1\x8f\xcc\x81; lit. dawn), also known as the <a href="/wiki/Functional_Cargo_Block" title="Functional Cargo Block">Functional Cargo Block</a> or FGB (from the Russian "\xd0\xa4\xd1\x83\xd0\xbd\xd0\xba\xd1\x86\xd0\xb8\xd0\xbe\xd0\xbd\xd0\xb0\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8c\xd0\xbd\xd0\xbe-\xd0\xb3\xd1\x80\xd1\x83\xd0\xb7\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb2\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb9 \xd0\xb1\xd0\xbb\xd0\xbe\xd0\xba", Funktsionalno-gruzovoy blok or \xd0\xa4\xd0\x93\xd0\x91), was the first module of the International Space Station to be launched. The FGB provided electrical power, storage, propulsion, and guidance to the ISS during the initial stage of assembly. With the launch and assembly in orbit of other modules with more specialised functionality, <i>Zarya</i> is now primarily used for storage, both inside the pressurised section and in the externally mounted fuel tanks. <i>Zarya</i> is a descendant of the <a href="/wiki/TKS_spacecraft" class="mw-redirect" title="TKS spacecraft">TKS spacecraft</a> designed for the Soviet <a href="/wiki/Salyut_programme" title="Salyut programme">Salyut programme</a>. The name <i>Zarya</i> was given to the FGB because it signified the dawn of a new era of international co-operation in space. Although it was built by a Russian company, it is owned by the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a>. <i>Zarya</i> weighs 19,300&#160;kg (42,500&#160;lb), is 12.55&#160;m (41.2&#160;ft) long and 4.1&#160;m (13&#160;ft) wide, discounting solar arrays.</p>\n<p><i>Zarya</i> was built from December 1994 to January 1998 at the <a href="/wiki/Khrunichev_State_Research_and_Production_Space_Center" title="Khrunichev State Research and Production Space Center">Khrunichev State Research and Production Space Center</a> (KhSC) in Moscow. The control system was developed by the Ukrainian <a href="/wiki/Khartron" title="Khartron">Khartron</a> corporation in <a href="/wiki/Kharkiv" title="Kharkiv">Kharkiv</a>.</p>\n<p><i>Zarya</i> was launched on 20 November 1998, on a Russian <a href="/wiki/Proton-K" title="Proton-K">Proton rocket</a> from <a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome_Site_81" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome Site 81">Baikonur Cosmodrome Site 81</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakhstan</a> to a 400&#160;km (250&#160;mi) high orbit with a designed lifetime of at least 15 years. After <i>Zarya</i> reached orbit, STS-88 launched on 4 December 1998, to attach the <i>Unity</i> module.</p>\n<p>Although only designed to fly autonomously for six to eight months, <i>Zarya</i> did so for almost two years because of delays with the Russian Service Module, <i>Zvezda</i>, which finally launched on 12 July 2000, and docked with <i>Zarya</i> on 26 July using the Russian <a href="/wiki/Kurs_(docking_system)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurs (docking system)">Kurs docking system</a>.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unity"><i>Unity</i></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Unity_module.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/ISS_Unity_module.jpg/220px-ISS_Unity_module.jpg" width="220" height="181" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/ISS_Unity_module.jpg/330px-ISS_Unity_module.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/ISS_Unity_module.jpg/440px-ISS_Unity_module.jpg 2x" data-file-width="816" data-file-height="672" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Unity_module.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i>Unity</i> as pictured by Space Shuttle <i>Endeavour</i></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Unity_(ISS_module)" title="Unity (ISS module)">Unity</a></b></i>, or Node 1, is one of three nodes, or passive connecting modules, in the <a href="/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment" title="US Orbital Segment">US Orbital Segment</a> of the station. It was the first US-built component of the Station to be launched. The module is made of aluminium and cylindrical in shape, with six berthing locations facilitating connections to other modules. Essential space station resources such as fluids, environmental control and life support systems, electrical and data systems are routed through <i>Unity</i> to supply work and living areas of the station. More than 50,000 mechanical items, 216 lines to carry fluids and gases, and 121 internal and external electrical cables using six miles of wire were installed in the <i>Unity</i> node. Prior to its launch, conical Pressurized Mating Adapters (PMAs) were attached to the aft and forward berthing mechanisms of <i>Unity</i>. <i>Unity</i> and the two mating adapters together weighed about 11,600&#160;kg (25,600&#160;lb). The adapters allow the docking systems used by the Space Shuttle and by Russian modules to attach to the node\'s hatches and berthing mechanisms.</p>\n<p><i>Unity</i> was carried into orbit by <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour"><i>Endeavour</i></a> in 1998 as the primary cargo of <a href="/wiki/STS-88" title="STS-88">STS-88</a>, the first Space Shuttle mission dedicated to assembly of the station. On 6 December 1998, the STS-88 crew mated the aft berthing port of <i>Unity</i> with the forward hatch of the already orbiting <i>Zarya</i> module.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zvezda"><i>Zvezda</i></span></h3>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)">Zvezda</a></b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x97\xd0\xb2\xd0\xb5\xd0\xb7\xd0\xb4\xd0\xb0\xcc\x81</span>, meaning "star"), also known as DOS-8, Service Module or SM (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\xa1\xd0\x9c</span>). Early in the station\'s life, <i>Zvezda</i> provided all of its critical systems.<sup id="cite_ref-huffpo20140714_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-huffpo20140714-93">[93]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-snasm20150819_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snasm20150819-94">[94]</a></sup> It made the station permanently habitable for the first time, adding life support for up to six crew and living quarters for two.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSBook_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSBook-10">[10]</a></sup> <i>Zvezda</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> DMS-R computer handles guidance, navigation and control for the entire space station.<sup id="cite_ref-Navigation_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Navigation-95">[95]</a></sup> A second computer which performs the same functions will be installed in the <i><a href="/wiki/Nauka_(ISS_module)" title="Nauka (ISS module)">Nauka</a></i> module, FGB-2.</p>\n<p>The hull of <i>Zvezda</i> was completed in February 1985, with major internal equipment installed by October 1986.<sup id="cite_ref-snasm20150819_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snasm20150819-94">[94]</a></sup> The module was launched by a Proton-K rocket from Site 81/23 at Baikonur, on 12 July 2000. <i>Zvezda</i> is at the rear of the station according to its normal direction of travel and orientation, and its engines may be used to boost the station\'s orbit. Alternatively Russian and European spacecraft can dock to <i>Zvezda</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> aft port and use their engines to boost the station.<sup id="cite_ref-nbcnews20070501_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbcnews20070501-96">[96]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-airspace20070301_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-airspace20070301-97">[97]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Destiny"><i>Destiny</i></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Destiny_as_just_installed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Destiny_as_just_installed.jpg/220px-Destiny_as_just_installed.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Destiny_as_just_installed.jpg/330px-Destiny_as_just_installed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Destiny_as_just_installed.jpg/440px-Destiny_as_just_installed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2992" data-file-height="1988" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Destiny_as_just_installed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i>Destiny</i> interior in February 2001</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Destiny_(ISS_module)" title="Destiny (ISS module)">Destiny</a></b></i>, also known as the U.S. Lab, is the primary research facility for United States payloads aboard the ISS. In 2011, NASA chose the not-for-profit group <a href="/wiki/Center_for_the_Advancement_of_Science_in_Space" title="Center for the Advancement of Science in Space">Center for the Advancement of Science in Space</a> (CASIS) to be the sole manager of all American science on the station which does not relate to manned exploration. The module houses 24 <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Payload_Rack" title="International Standard Payload Rack">International Standard Payload Racks</a>, some of which are used for environmental systems and crew daily living equipment. <i>Destiny</i> also serves as the mounting point for the station\'s Truss Structure.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Quest"><i>Quest</i></span></h3>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Quest_Joint_Airlock" title="Quest Joint Airlock">Quest</a></b></i> is the only USOS airlock, and hosts spacewalks with both United States <a href="/wiki/Extravehicular_Mobility_Unit" title="Extravehicular Mobility Unit">EMU</a> and Russian <a href="/wiki/Orlan_space_suit" title="Orlan space suit">Orlan</a> <a href="/wiki/Space_suit" title="Space suit">spacesuits</a>. It consists of two segments: the equipment lock, which stores spacesuits and equipment, and the crew lock, from which astronauts can exit into space. This module has a separately controlled atmosphere. Crew sleep in this module, breathing a low nitrogen mixture the night before scheduled EVAs, to avoid <a href="/wiki/Decompression_sickness" title="Decompression sickness">decompression sickness</a> (known as "the bends") in the low-pressure suits.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pirs_and_Poisk"><i>Pirs</i> and <i>Poisk</i></span></h3>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Pirs_(ISS_module)" title="Pirs (ISS module)">Pirs</a></b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x9f\xd0\xb8\xd1\x80\xd1\x81</span>, meaning "<a href="/wiki/Pier" title="Pier">pier</a>"), (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\xa1\xd1\x82\xd1\x8b\xd0\xba\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb2\xd0\xbe\xd1\x87\xd0\xbd\xd1\x8b\xd0\xb9 \xd0\xbe\xd1\x82\xd1\x81\xd0\xb5\xd0\xba</span>), "docking module", SO-1 or DC-1 (docking compartment), and <i><b><a href="/wiki/Poisk_(ISS_module)" title="Poisk (ISS module)">Poisk</a></b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x9f\xd0\xbe\xcc\x81\xd0\xb8\xd1\x81\xd0\xba</span>; lit. <i>Search</i>), also known as the <a href="/wiki/Mini-Research_Module" title="Mini-Research Module">Mini-Research Module</a> 2 (MRM 2), <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru"><i>\xd0\x9c\xd0\xb0\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8b\xd0\xb9 \xd0\xb8\xd1\x81\xd1\x81\xd0\xbb\xd0\xb5\xd0\xb4\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb2\xd0\xb0\xd1\x82\xd0\xb5\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8c\xd1\x81\xd0\xba\xd0\xb8\xd0\xb9 \xd0\xbc\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb4\xd1\x83\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8c 2</i></span>, or \xd0\x9c\xd0\x98\xd0\x9c 2. <i>Pirs</i> and <i>Poisk</i> are Russian airlock modules, each having 2 identical hatches. An outward-opening hatch on the <i>Mir</i> space station failed after it swung open too fast after unlatching, because of a small amount of air pressure remaining in the airlock.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> A different entry was used, and the hatch was repaired. All EVA hatches on the ISS open inwards and are pressure-sealing. <i>Pirs</i> was used to store, service, and refurbish Russian <a href="/wiki/Orlan_suits" class="mw-redirect" title="Orlan suits">Orlan suits</a> and provided contingency entry for crew using the slightly bulkier American suits. The outermost docking ports on both airlocks allow docking of Soyuz and Progress spacecraft, and the automatic transfer of propellants to and from storage on the ROS.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harmony"><i>Harmony</i></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:369px;max-width:369px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:173px;max-width:173px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg/171px-Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg" width="171" height="175" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg/257px-Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg/342px-Node_2_-_STS-134.jpg 2x" data-file-width="804" data-file-height="822" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Harmony</i> node in 2011</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:192px;max-width:192px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg/190px-Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg" width="190" height="174" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg/285px-Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg/380px-Node_3_-_Isolated_view.jpg 2x" data-file-width="448" data-file-height="411" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><i>Tranquility</i> node in 2011</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Harmony_(ISS_module)" title="Harmony (ISS module)">Harmony</a></b></i>, also known as Node 2, is the second of the station\'s node modules and the utility hub of the USOS. The module contains four racks that provide electrical power, bus electronic data, and acts as a central connecting point for several other components via its six Common Berthing Mechanisms (CBMs). The European <i>Columbus</i> and Japanese <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> laboratories are permanently berthed to the starboard and port radial ports respectively. The nadir and zenith ports can be used for docking visiting spacecraft including HTV, Dragon, and Cygnus, with the nadir port serving as the primary docking port. American Shuttle Orbiters docked with the ISS via PMA-2, attached to the forward port.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tranquility"><i>Tranquility</i></span></h3>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Tranquility_(ISS_module)" title="Tranquility (ISS module)">Tranquility</a></b></i>, also known as Node 3, is the third and last of the station\'s US nodes, it contains an additional life support system to recycle waste water for crew use and supplements oxygen generation. Like the other US nodes, it has six berthing mechanisms, five of which are currently in use. The first one connects to the station\'s core via the <i><a href="/wiki/Unity_(ISS_module)" title="Unity (ISS module)">Unity</a></i> module, others host the <i><a href="/wiki/Cupola_(ISS_module)" title="Cupola (ISS module)">Cupola</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Pressurized_Mating_Adapter" title="Pressurized Mating Adapter">PMA docking port</a> #3, the <a href="/wiki/Leonardo_(ISS_module)" title="Leonardo (ISS module)"><i>Leonardo</i> PMM</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Expandable_Activity_Module" title="Bigelow Expandable Activity Module">Bigelow Expandable Activity Module</a>. The final zenith port remains free.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Columbus"><i>Columbus</i></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:S122e008264.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/S122e008264.jpg/220px-S122e008264.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/S122e008264.jpg/330px-S122e008264.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/S122e008264.jpg/440px-S122e008264.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2000" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:S122e008264.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i>Columbus</i> module in 2008</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Columbus_(ISS_module)" title="Columbus (ISS module)">Columbus</a></b></i>, the primary research facility for European payloads aboard the ISS, provides a <a href="/wiki/European_Drawer_Rack" title="European Drawer Rack">generic laboratory</a> as well as facilities specifically designed for <a href="/wiki/Biolab" title="Biolab">biology</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_Physiology_Modules" title="European Physiology Modules">biomedical research</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fluid_Science_Laboratory" title="Fluid Science Laboratory">fluid physics</a>. Several mounting locations are affixed to the exterior of the module, which provide power and data to external experiments such as the <a href="/wiki/European_Technology_Exposure_Facility" title="European Technology Exposure Facility">European Technology Exposure Facility</a> (EuTEF), <a href="/wiki/Solar_Monitoring_Observatory" class="mw-redirect" title="Solar Monitoring Observatory">Solar Monitoring Observatory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Materials_International_Space_Station_Experiment" title="Materials International Space Station Experiment">Materials International Space Station Experiment</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Clock_Ensemble_in_Space" title="Atomic Clock Ensemble in Space">Atomic Clock Ensemble in Space</a>. A number of expansions are planned for the module to study <a href="/wiki/Quantum_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Quantum physics">quantum physics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cosmology" title="Cosmology">cosmology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> ESA\'s development of technologies on all the main areas of life support has been ongoing for more than 20 years and are/have been used in modules such as <i>Columbus</i> and the ATV. The German Aerospace Center <a href="/wiki/German_Aerospace_Center" title="German Aerospace Center">DLR</a> manages ground control operations for <i>Columbus</i> and the ATV is controlled from the French <a href="/wiki/CNES" title="CNES">CNES</a> <a href="/wiki/Toulouse_Space_Center" title="Toulouse Space Center">Toulouse Space Center</a>.</p>\n<h3><span id="Kib\xc5\x8d"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Kib.C5.8D"><i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:%E3%81%8D%E3%81%BC%E3%81%86_%E3%82%A8%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AD%E3%83%83%E3%82%AF_Kibo_airlock.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E3%81%8D%E3%81%BC%E3%81%86_%E3%82%A8%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AD%E3%83%83%E3%82%AF_Kibo_airlock.jpg/220px-%E3%81%8D%E3%81%BC%E3%81%86_%E3%82%A8%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AD%E3%83%83%E3%82%AF_Kibo_airlock.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E3%81%8D%E3%81%BC%E3%81%86_%E3%82%A8%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AD%E3%83%83%E3%82%AF_Kibo_airlock.jpg/330px-%E3%81%8D%E3%81%BC%E3%81%86_%E3%82%A8%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AD%E3%83%83%E3%82%AF_Kibo_airlock.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/%E3%81%8D%E3%81%BC%E3%81%86_%E3%82%A8%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AD%E3%83%83%E3%82%AF_Kibo_airlock.jpg/440px-%E3%81%8D%E3%81%BC%E3%81%86_%E3%82%A8%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AD%E3%83%83%E3%82%AF_Kibo_airlock.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3068" data-file-height="2040" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:%E3%81%8D%E3%81%BC%E3%81%86_%E3%82%A8%E3%82%A2%E3%83%AD%E3%83%83%E3%82%AF_Kibo_airlock.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nNot large enough for crew using spacesuits, the airlock on <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> has a sliding drawer for external experiments.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Kibo_(ISS_module)" title="Kibo (ISS module)">Kib\xc5\x8d</a></b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a>: <span lang="ja" xml:lang="ja">\xe3\x81\x8d\xe3\x81\xbc\xe3\x81\x86</span>, "<a href="/wiki/Hope" title="Hope">hope</a>") is a laboratory and the largest ISS module. It is used to do research in space medicine, biology, Earth observations, materials production, biotechnology and communications, and has facilities for growing plants and fish. During August 2011, the <a href="/wiki/MAXI_(ISS_Experiment)" title="MAXI (ISS Experiment)">MAXI</a> observatory mounted on <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i>, which uses the ISS\'s orbital motion to image the whole sky in the X-ray spectrum, detected for the first time the moment when a star was swallowed by a black hole.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> The laboratory contains 23 racks, including 10 experiment racks, and has a dedicated airlock for experiments. In a \'shirt sleeves\' environment, crew attach an experiment to the sliding drawer within the airlock, close the inner, and then open the outer hatch. By extending the drawer and removing the experiment using the dedicated robotic arm, payloads are placed on the external platform. The process can be reversed and repeated quickly, allowing access to maintain external experiments without the delays caused by EVAs.</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-nolines">\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 120px">\n<div style="width: 120px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 115px;">\n<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kibo_-_Pressurized_Module.jpg" Module</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 120px">\n<div style="width: 120px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 115px;">\n<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a
