srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_%28Pressurized_Section%29.jpg/173px-Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_%28Pressurized_Section%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_%28Pressurized_Section%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="180" data-file-height="144" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Experiment Logistics Module</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 120px">\n<div style="width: 120px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 115px;">\n<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kibo_-_Exposed_Facility.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Kibo_-_Exposed_Facility.jpg/115px-Kibo_-_Exposed_Facility.jpg" width="115" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Kibo_-_Exposed_Facility.jpg/173px-Kibo_-_Exposed_Facility.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fc/Kibo_-_Exposed_Facility.jpg 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="163" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Exposed Facility</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 120px">\n<div style="width: 120px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 115px;">\n<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_(Exposed_Section).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_%28Exposed_Section%29.jpg/115px-Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_%28Exposed_Section%29.jpg" width="115" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_%28Exposed_Section%29.jpg/173px-Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_%28Exposed_Section%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/14/Kibo_-_Experiment_Logistics_Module_%28Exposed_Section%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="163" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Experiment Logistics Module</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</li>\n<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 120px">\n<div style="width: 120px">\n<div class="thumb" style="width: 115px;">\n<div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kibo_-_Remote_Manipulator_System.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Kibo_-_Remote_Manipulator_System.jpg/115px-Kibo_-_Remote_Manipulator_System.jpg" width="115" height="94" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Kibo_-_Remote_Manipulator_System.jpg/173px-Kibo_-_Remote_Manipulator_System.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/97/Kibo_-_Remote_Manipulator_System.jpg 2x" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="163" /></a></div>\n</div>\n<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Remote Manipulator System</p>\n</div>\n</div>\n</li>\n</ul>\n<p>A smaller pressurised module is attached to the top of <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i>, serving as a cargo bay. The dedicated Interorbital Communications System (ICS) allows large amounts of data to be beamed from <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> ICS, first to the Japanese KODAMA satellite in geostationary orbit, then to Japanese ground stations. When a direct communication link is used, contact time between the ISS and a ground station is limited to approximately 10 minutes per visible pass. When KODAMA relays data between a LEO spacecraft and a ground station, real-time communications are possible in 60% of the flight path of the spacecraft. Japanese ground controllers use <a href="/wiki/Telepresence_robotics" class="mw-redirect" title="Telepresence robotics">telepresence robotics</a> to remotely conduct onboard research and experiments, thus reducing the workload of station astronauts. Ground controllers also use a free-floating autonomous <a href="/wiki/Int-Ball" title="Int-Ball">ball camera</a> to photodocument astronaut and space station activities, further freeing up astronaut time.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cupola">Cupola</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:388px;max-width:388px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:192px;max-width:192px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:STS130_cupola_view1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/STS130_cupola_view1.jpg/190px-STS130_cupola_view1.jpg" width="190" height="126" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/STS130_cupola_view1.jpg/285px-STS130_cupola_view1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/STS130_cupola_view1.jpg/380px-STS130_cupola_view1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The <a href="/wiki/Cupola_(ISS_module)" title="Cupola (ISS module)">Cupola</a>\'s design has been compared to the <i><a href="/wiki/Millennium_Falcon" title="Millennium Falcon">Millennium Falcon</a></i> from <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">Star Wars</a></i>.</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:192px;max-width:192px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS-27_Dmitri_Kondratyev_and_Paolo_Nespoli_photograph_the_Earth_through_the_Cupola.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/ISS-27_Dmitri_Kondratyev_and_Paolo_Nespoli_photograph_the_Earth_through_the_Cupola.jpg/190px-ISS-27_Dmitri_Kondratyev_and_Paolo_Nespoli_photograph_the_Earth_through_the_Cupola.jpg" width="190" height="126" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/ISS-27_Dmitri_Kondratyev_and_Paolo_Nespoli_photograph_the_Earth_through_the_Cupola.jpg/285px-ISS-27_Dmitri_Kondratyev_and_Paolo_Nespoli_photograph_the_Earth_through_the_Cupola.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/ISS-27_Dmitri_Kondratyev_and_Paolo_Nespoli_photograph_the_Earth_through_the_Cupola.jpg/380px-ISS-27_Dmitri_Kondratyev_and_Paolo_Nespoli_photograph_the_Earth_through_the_Cupola.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Dmitri_Kondratyev" title="Dmitri Kondratyev">Dmitri Kondratyev</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paolo_A._Nespoli" class="mw-redirect" title="Paolo A. Nespoli">Paolo Nespoli</a> in the Cupola. Background left to right, <a href="/wiki/Progress_M-09M" title="Progress M-09M">Progress M-09M</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_TMA-20" title="Soyuz TMA-20">Soyuz TMA-20</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Leonardo_(ISS_module)" title="Leonardo (ISS module)">Leonardo</a></i> module and <a href="/wiki/Kounotori_2" title="Kounotori 2">HTV-2</a>.</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><b><a href="/wiki/Cupola_(ISS_module)" title="Cupola (ISS module)">Cupola</a></b> is a seven-window observatory, used to view Earth and docking spacecraft. Its name derives from the Italian word <i>cupola</i>, which means "dome". The Cupola project was started by NASA and Boeing, but cancelled due to budget cuts. A barter agreement between NASA and ESA led to ESA resuming development of Cupola in 1998. It was built by Thales Alenia Space in Turin, Italy. The module comes equipped with robotic workstations for operating the station\'s main robotic arm and shutters to protect its windows from damage caused by micrometeorites. It features 7 windows, with an 80-centimetre (31&#160;in) round window, the largest window on the station (and the largest flown in space to date). The distinctive design has been compared to the \'turret\' of the fictitious <i><a href="/wiki/Millennium_Falcon" title="Millennium Falcon">Millennium Falcon</a></i> from the motion picture <i><a href="/wiki/Star_Wars_(film)" title="Star Wars (film)">Star Wars</a></i>;<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> the original prop <a href="/wiki/Lightsaber" title="Lightsaber">lightsaber</a> used by actor <a href="/wiki/Mark_Hamill" title="Mark Hamill">Mark Hamill</a> as <a href="/wiki/Luke_Skywalker" title="Luke Skywalker">Luke Skywalker</a> in the 1977 film was flown to the station in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rassvet"><i>Rassvet</i></span></h3>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Rassvet_(ISS_module)" title="Rassvet (ISS module)">Rassvet</a></b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\xa0\xd0\xb0\xd1\x81\xd1\x81\xd0\xb2\xd0\xb5\xcc\x81\xd1\x82</span>; lit. "dawn"), also known as the Mini-Research Module 1 (MRM-1) (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x9c\xd0\xb0\xcc\x81\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8b\xd0\xb9 \xd0\xb8\xd1\x81\xd1\x81\xd0\xbb\xd0\xb5\xcc\x81\xd0\xb4\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb2\xd0\xb0\xd1\x82\xd0\xb5\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8c\xd1\x81\xd0\xba\xd0\xb8\xd0\xb9 \xd0\xbc\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb4\xd1\x83\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8c</span>, <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x9c\xd0\x98\xd0\x9c 1</span>) and formerly known as the Docking Cargo Module (DCM), is similar in design to the <a href="/wiki/Mir_Docking_Module" title="Mir Docking Module"><i>Mir</i> Docking Module</a> launched on <a href="/wiki/STS-74" title="STS-74">STS-74</a> in 1995. <i>Rassvet</i> is primarily used for cargo storage and for docking by visiting spacecraft. It was flown to the ISS aboard NASA\'s <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Atlantis" title="Space Shuttle Atlantis"><i>Atlantis</i></a> on the <a href="/wiki/STS-132" title="STS-132">STS-132</a> mission and connected in May 2010,<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup> <i>Rassvet</i> is the only Russian-owned module launched by NASA, to repay for the launch of <i>Zarya</i>, which is Russian designed and built, but partially paid for by NASA.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup> <i>Rassvet</i> was launched with the Russian <i>Nauka</i> laboratory\'s experiments airlock temporarily attached to it, and spare parts for the European Robotic Arm.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Leonardo"><i>Leonardo</i></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-133_Installation_PMM_3.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/STS-133_Installation_PMM_3.jpg/220px-STS-133_Installation_PMM_3.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/STS-133_Installation_PMM_3.jpg/330px-STS-133_Installation_PMM_3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/STS-133_Installation_PMM_3.jpg/440px-STS-133_Installation_PMM_3.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4256" data-file-height="2832" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-133_Installation_PMM_3.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i>Leonardo</i> installed</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><b><i><a href="/wiki/Leonardo_(ISS_module)" title="Leonardo (ISS module)">Leonardo</a></i> Permanent Multipurpose Module</b> (PMM) is a storage module attached to the <i><a href="/wiki/Tranquility_(ISS_module)" title="Tranquility (ISS module)">Tranquility</a></i> node.<sup id="cite_ref-leo-esa20110301_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-leo-esa20110301-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> The three NASA Space Shuttle <a href="/wiki/Multi-Purpose_Logistics_Module" title="Multi-Purpose Logistics Module">MPLM</a> cargo containers\xe2\x80\x94<i>Leonardo</i>, <i>Raffaello</i> and <i>Donatello</i>\xe2\x80\x94were built for NASA in <a href="/wiki/Turin" title="Turin">Turin</a>, Italy by Alcatel Alenia Space, now <a href="/wiki/Thales_Alenia_Space" title="Thales Alenia Space">Thales Alenia Space</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> The MPLMs were provided to NASA\'s ISS programme by Italy (independent of their role as a member state of ESA) and are considered to be US elements. In a bartered exchange for providing these containers, the US gave Italy research time aboard the ISS out of the US allotment in addition to that which Italy receives as a member of ESA.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The Permanent Multipurpose Module was created by converting <i>Leonardo</i> into a module that could be permanently attached to the station.<sup id="cite_ref-PLM1_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PLM1-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-PLM2_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PLM2-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bigelow_Expandable_Activity_Module">Bigelow Expandable Activity Module</span></h3>\n<p><b><a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Expandable_Activity_Module" title="Bigelow Expandable Activity Module">Bigelow Expandable Activity Module</a></b> (BEAM) is a prototype <a href="/wiki/Inflatable_space_habitat" title="Inflatable space habitat">inflatable space habitat</a> serving as a two-year technology demonstration.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup> It was built by <a href="/wiki/Bigelow_Aerospace" title="Bigelow Aerospace">Bigelow Aerospace</a> under a contract established by NASA on 16 January 2013. BEAM was delivered to the ISS aboard <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_CRS-8" title="SpaceX CRS-8">SpaceX CRS-8</a> on 10 April 2016, was berthed to the aft port of the <i><a href="/wiki/Tranquility_(ISS_module)" title="Tranquility (ISS module)">Tranquility</a></i> node on 16 April,<sup id="cite_ref-space20160410_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-space20160410-120">[120]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sfnow20160416_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sfnow20160416-121">[121]</a></sup> and was fully expanded on 28 May.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>\n<p>During its two-year test run, instruments are measuring its structural integrity and leak rate, along with temperature and radiation levels. The hatch leading into the module remains closed except for periodic visits by space station crew members for inspections and data collection. The module was originally planned to be jettisoned from the station following the test,<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> but following positive data after a year in orbit, NASA has suggested that it could remain on the station as a storage area.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scheduled_additional_modules">Scheduled additional modules</span></h3>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Nauka"><i>Nauka</i></span></h4>\n<p><i><b><a href="/wiki/Nauka_(ISS_module)" title="Nauka (ISS module)">Nauka</a></b></i> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x9d\xd0\xb0\xd1\x83\xcc\x81\xd0\xba\xd0\xb0</span>; lit. "science"), also known as the Multipurpose Laboratory Module (MLM) or FGB-2 (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x9c\xd0\xbd\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb3\xd0\xbe\xd1\x84\xd1\x83\xd0\xbd\xd0\xba\xd1\x86\xd0\xb8\xd0\xbe\xd0\xbd\xd0\xb0\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8c\xd0\xbd\xd1\x8b\xd0\xb9 \xd0\xbb\xd0\xb0\xd0\xb1\xd0\xbe\xd1\x80\xd0\xb0\xd1\x82\xd0\xbe\xd1\x80\xd0\xbd\xd1\x8b\xd0\xb9 \xd0\xbc\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb4\xd1\x83\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8c</span>, <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x9c\xd0\x9b\xd0\x9c</span>), is the major Russian laboratory module. It was scheduled to arrive at the station in 2014, docking to the port that was occupied by the <i>Pirs</i> module.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Due to deterioration during many years spent in storage, it proved necessary to build a new propulsion module,<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> and the launch date was postponed to 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Before the <i>Nauka</i> module arrives, a Progress spacecraft will remove <i>Pirs</i> from the station and deorbit it to reenter over the Pacific Ocean. <i>Nauka</i> contains an additional set of life support systems and attitude control. Originally it would have routed power from the single Science-and-Power Platform, but that single module design changed over the first ten years of the ISS mission, and the two science modules, which attach to <i>Nauka</i> via the <a href="/wiki/Uzlovoy_Module" title="Uzlovoy Module">Uzlovoy Module</a>, or Russian node, each incorporate their own large solar arrays to power Russian science experiments in the ROS.</p>\n<p><i>Nauka</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> mission has changed over time. During the mid-1990s, it was intended as a backup for the FGB, and later as a universal docking module (UDM); its docking ports will be able to support automatic docking of both spacecraft, additional modules and fuel transfer. <i>Nauka</i> has its own engines. Like <i>Zvezda</i> and <i>Zarya</i>, <i>Nauka</i> will be launched by a Proton rocket, while smaller Russian modules such as <i>Pirs</i> and <i>Poisk</i> were delivered by modified <a href="/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft)" title="Progress (spacecraft)">Progress</a> spacecraft. Russia plans to separate <i>Nauka</i>, along with the rest of the Russian Orbital Segment, to form the <a href="/wiki/OPSEK" class="mw-redirect" title="OPSEK">OPSEK</a> space station before the ISS is deorbited.</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Uzlovoy_Module">Uzlovoy Module</span></h4>\n<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/Uzlovoy_Module" title="Uzlovoy Module">Uzlovoy Module</a></b> (UM), or Node Module is a 4-metric-ton<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> ball-shaped module that will allow docking of two scientific and power modules during the final stage of the station assembly, and provide the Russian segment additional docking ports to receive Soyuz MS and Progress MS spacecraft. UM is due to be launched in late 2018. It will be integrated with a special version of the Progress cargo ship and launched by a standard Soyuz rocket. Progress would use its own propulsion and flight control system to deliver and dock the Node Module to the nadir (Earth-facing) docking port of the <i>Nauka</i> MLM/FGB-2 module. One port is equipped with an active hybrid docking port, which enables docking with the MLM module. The remaining five ports are passive hybrids, enabling docking of Soyuz and Progress vehicles, as well as heavier modules and future spacecraft with modified docking systems. The node module was conceived to serve as the only permanent element of the future Russian successor to the ISS, <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Piloted_Assembly_and_Experiment_Complex" title="Orbital Piloted Assembly and Experiment Complex">OPSEK</a>. Equipped with six docking ports, the Node Module would serve as a single permanent core of the future station with all other modules coming and going as their life span and mission required.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> This would be a progression beyond the ISS and Russia\'s modular <i>Mir</i> space station, which are in turn more advanced than early monolithic first generation stations such as <i>Skylab</i>, and early <i>Salyut</i> and <i>Almaz</i> stations.</p>\n<p><b>Science Power Modules 1 &amp; 2</b> (<a href="/wiki/Science-Power_Module-1" title="Science-Power Module-1">NEM-1</a>, NEM-2) (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\x9d\xd0\xb0\xd1\x83\xcc\x81\xd1\x87\xd0\xbd\xd0\xbe-\xd0\xad\xd0\xbd\xd0\xb5\xd1\x80\xd0\xb3\xd0\xb5\xd1\x82\xd0\xb8\xd1\x87\xd0\xb5\xd1\x81\xd0\xba\xd0\xb8\xd0\xb9 \xd0\x9c\xd0\xbe\xd0\xb4\xd1\x83\xd0\xbb\xd1\x8c-1 \xd0\xb8 -2</span>)</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="NanoRacks_Airlock_Module">NanoRacks Airlock Module</span></h4>\n<p>The <b><a href="/wiki/NanoRacks" title="NanoRacks">NanoRacks</a> Airlock Module</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Commercial_use_of_space" title="Commercial use of space">commercially</a>-funded <a href="/wiki/Airlock" title="Airlock">airlock</a> module intended to be launched in 2019. The module is being built by NanoRacks and <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-NanoRacks_Airlock_Module_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NanoRacks_Airlock_Module-131">[131]</a></sup> and will be used to deploy <a href="/wiki/CubeSat" title="CubeSat">CubeSats</a>, small satellites, and other external payloads for <a href="/wiki/NASA" title="NASA">NASA</a>, <a href="/wiki/CASIS" class="mw-redirect" title="CASIS">CASIS</a>, and other commercial and governmental customers. It is intended to be manifested with a <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Resupply_Services" title="Commercial Resupply Services">Commercial Resupply Services</a> mission.<sup id="cite_ref-NASA_NanoRacks_Announcement_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA_NanoRacks_Announcement-132">[132]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Habitation_module.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/ISS_Habitation_module.jpg/220px-ISS_Habitation_module.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/ISS_Habitation_module.jpg/330px-ISS_Habitation_module.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/ISS_Habitation_module.jpg/440px-ISS_Habitation_module.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="1212" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Habitation_module.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe cancelled Habitation module under construction in 1997</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancelled_components">Cancelled components</span></h3>\n<p>Several modules planned for the station were cancelled over the course of the ISS programme. Reasons include budgetary constraints, the modules becoming unnecessary, and station redesigns after the 2003 <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster"><i>Columbia</i> disaster</a>. The US <a href="/wiki/Centrifuge_Accommodations_Module" title="Centrifuge Accommodations Module">Centrifuge Accommodations Module</a> would have hosted science experiments in varying levels of <a href="/wiki/Artificial_gravity" title="Artificial gravity">artificial gravity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> The US <a href="/wiki/Habitation_Module" title="Habitation Module">Habitation Module</a> would have served as the station\'s living quarters. Instead, the sleep stations are now spread throughout the station.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup> The US <a href="/wiki/Interim_Control_Module" title="Interim Control Module">Interim Control Module</a> and <a href="/wiki/ISS_Propulsion_Module" title="ISS Propulsion Module">ISS Propulsion Module</a> would have replaced the functions of <i>Zvezda</i> in case of a launch failure.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup> Two <a href="/wiki/Russian_Research_Module" title="Russian Research Module">Russian Research Modules</a> were planned for scientific research.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> They would have docked to a Russian <a href="/wiki/Universal_Docking_Module" title="Universal Docking Module">Universal Docking Module</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> The Russian <a href="/wiki/Science_Power_Platform" title="Science Power Platform">Science Power Platform</a> would have supplied power to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment" title="Russian Orbital Segment">Russian Orbital Segment</a> independent of the ITS solar arrays.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Unpressurised_elements">Unpressurised elements</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Truss_breakdown.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Truss_breakdown.png/220px-Truss_breakdown.png" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Truss_breakdown.png/330px-Truss_breakdown.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Truss_breakdown.png/440px-Truss_breakdown.png 2x" data-file-width="2513" data-file-height="1650" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Truss_breakdown.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nISS Truss Components breakdown showing Trusses and all ORUs in situ</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The ISS has a large number of external components that do not require pressurisation. The largest of these is the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure" title="Integrated Truss Structure">Integrated Truss Structure</a> (ITS), to which the station\'s main solar arrays and thermal radiators are mounted.<sup id="cite_ref-Arrays_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arrays-138">[138]</a></sup> The ITS consists of ten separate segments forming a structure 108.5&#160;m (356&#160;ft) long.<sup id="cite_ref-OnOrbit_5-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OnOrbit-5">[5]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The station in its complete form has several smaller external components, such as the six robotic arms, the three <a href="/wiki/External_Stowage_Platform" class="mw-redirect" title="External Stowage Platform">External Stowage Platforms</a> (ESPs) and four <a href="/wiki/ExPRESS_Logistics_Carrier" title="ExPRESS Logistics Carrier">ExPRESS Logistics Carriers</a> (ELCs).<sup id="cite_ref-Manifest_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Manifest-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> While these platforms allow experiments (including <a href="/wiki/MISSE" class="mw-redirect" title="MISSE">MISSE</a>, the STP-H3 and the <a href="/wiki/Robotic_Refueling_Mission" title="Robotic Refueling Mission">Robotic Refueling Mission</a>) to be deployed and conducted in the vacuum of space by providing electricity and processing experimental data locally, their primary function is to store spare <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Replacement_Unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbital Replacement Unit">Orbital Replacement Units</a> (ORUs). ORUs are parts that can be replaced when they fail or pass their design life. Examples of ORUs include pumps, storage tanks, antennas and battery units. Such units are replaced either by astronauts during EVA or by robotic arms. Spare parts were routinely transported to and from the station via Space Shuttle resupply missions, with a heavy emphasis on ORU transport once the NASA Shuttle approached retirement.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Several shuttle missions were dedicated to the delivery of ORUs, including <a href="/wiki/STS-129" title="STS-129">STS-129</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-EVA129_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EVA129-142">[142]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/STS-133" title="STS-133">STS-133</a><sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/STS-134" title="STS-134">STS-134</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> As of January 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, only one other mode of transportation of ORUs had been utilised \xe2\x80\x93 the Japanese cargo vessel <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">HTV-2</a> \xe2\x80\x93 which delivered an FHRC and CTC-2 via its Exposed Pallet (EP).<sup id="cite_ref-HTV2_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HTV2-145">[145]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Precise_language" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The text near this tag is dated.">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg/220px-STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg" width="220" height="145" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg/330px-STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg/440px-STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2000" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-116_spacewalk_1.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nConstruction of the <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure" title="Integrated Truss Structure">Integrated Truss Structure</a> over New Zealand.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>There are also smaller exposure facilities mounted directly to laboratory modules; the <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Experiment_Module#Exposed_Facility" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Experiment Module">Exposed Facility</a> serves as an external \'<a href="/wiki/Porch" title="Porch">porch</a>\' for the <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> complex,<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup> and a facility on the European <i>Columbus</i> laboratory provides power and data connections for experiments such as the <a href="/wiki/European_Technology_Exposure_Facility" title="European Technology Exposure Facility">European Technology Exposure Facility</a><sup id="cite_ref-NASA_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASA-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ESA_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESA-148">[148]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Atomic_Clock_Ensemble_in_Space" title="Atomic Clock Ensemble in Space">Atomic Clock Ensemble in Space</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Remote_sensing" title="Remote sensing">remote sensing</a> instrument, <a href="/wiki/Stratospheric_Aerosol_and_Gas_Experiment" title="Stratospheric Aerosol and Gas Experiment">SAGE III-ISS</a>, was delivered to the station in 2014 aboard a <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon">Dragon capsule</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Neutron_star_Interior_Composition_ExploreR" class="mw-redirect" title="Neutron star Interior Composition ExploreR">NICER</a> experiment is scheduled to be delivered in 2016.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup> The largest such scientific payload externally mounted to the ISS is the <a href="/wiki/Alpha_Magnetic_Spectrometer" title="Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer">Alpha Magnetic Spectrometer</a> (AMS), a particle physics experiment launched on <a href="/wiki/STS-134" title="STS-134">STS-134</a> in May 2011, and mounted externally on the ITS. The AMS measures <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> to look for evidence of <a href="/wiki/Dark_matter" title="Dark matter">dark matter</a> and <a href="/wiki/Antimatter" title="Antimatter">antimatter</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Robotic_arms_and_cargo_cranes">Robotic arms and cargo cranes</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:508px;max-width:508px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Iss017e011097.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Iss017e011097.jpg/250px-Iss017e011097.jpg" width="250" height="166" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Iss017e011097.jpg/375px-Iss017e011097.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Iss017e011097.jpg/500px-Iss017e011097.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2012" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Commander <a href="/wiki/Sergey_Alexandrovich_Volkov" class="mw-redirect" title="Sergey Alexandrovich Volkov">Volkov</a> stands on <i>Pirs</i> with his back to the <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_spacecraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Soyuz spacecraft">Soyuz</a> whilst operating the manual <a href="/wiki/Strela_(crane)" title="Strela (crane)">Strela crane</a> holding photographer <a href="/wiki/Oleg_Kononenko" title="Oleg Kononenko">Kononenko</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Zarya" title="Zarya">Zarya</a></i> is seen to the left and <i><a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)">Zvezda</a></i> across the bottom of the image.</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:252px;max-width:252px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg/250px-Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg" width="250" height="166" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg/375px-Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg/500px-Dextrereallyhasnohead.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2009" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Dextre" title="Dextre">Dextre</a>, like many of the station\'s experiments and robotic arms, can be operated from Earth and perform tasks while the crew sleeps.</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Integrated_Truss_Structure" title="Integrated Truss Structure">Integrated Truss Structure</a> serves as a base for the station\'s primary remote manipulator system, called the <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Servicing_System" title="Mobile Servicing System">Mobile Servicing System</a> (MSS), which is composed of three main components. <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Servicing_System#Canadarm2" title="Mobile Servicing System">Canadarm2</a>, the largest robotic arm on the ISS, has a mass of 1,800 kilograms (4,000&#160;lb) and is used to dock and manipulate spacecraft and modules on the USOS, hold crew members and equipment in place during EVAs and move Dextre around to perform tasks.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Dextre" title="Dextre">Dextre</a> is a 1,560&#160;kg (3,440&#160;lb) robotic manipulator with two arms, a rotating torso and has power tools, lights and video for replacing <a href="/wiki/Orbital_replacement_unit" title="Orbital replacement unit">orbital replacement units</a> (ORUs) and performing other tasks requiring fine control.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Base_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Mobile Base System">Mobile Base System</a> (MBS) is a platform which rides on rails along the length of the station\'s main truss. It serves as a mobile base for Canadarm2 and Dextre, allowing the robotic arms to reach all parts of the USOS.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup> To gain access to the Russian Segment a <a href="/wiki/Grapple_Fixture" class="mw-redirect" title="Grapple Fixture">grapple fixture</a> was added to <i>Zarya</i> on <a href="/wiki/STS-134" title="STS-134">STS-134</a>, so that Canadarm2 can inchworm itself onto the ROS.<sup id="cite_ref-presskit134_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presskit134-156">[156]</a></sup> Also installed during STS-134 was the 15&#160;m (50&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Orbiter_Boom_Sensor_System" title="Orbiter Boom Sensor System">Orbiter Boom Sensor System</a> (OBSS), which had been used to inspect heat shield tiles on Space Shuttle missions and can be used on station to increase the reach of the MSS.<sup id="cite_ref-presskit134_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-presskit134-156">[156]</a></sup> Staff on Earth or the station can operate the MSS components via remote control, performing work outside the station without space walks.</p>\n<p>Japan\'s <a href="/wiki/Japanese_Remote_Manipulator_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Japanese Remote Manipulator System">Remote Manipulator System</a>, which services the <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> Exposed Facility,<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup> was launched on <a href="/wiki/STS-124" title="STS-124">STS-124</a> and is attached to the <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> Pressurised Module.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">[158]</a></sup> The arm is similar to the Space Shuttle arm as it is permanently attached at one end and has a latching end effector for standard grapple fixtures at the other.</p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/European_Robotic_Arm" title="European Robotic Arm">European Robotic Arm</a>, which will service the Russian Orbital Segment, will be launched alongside the <a href="/wiki/Multipurpose_Laboratory_Module" class="mw-redirect" title="Multipurpose Laboratory Module">Multipurpose Laboratory Module</a> in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup> The ROS does not require spacecraft or modules to be manipulated, as all spacecraft and modules dock automatically and may be discarded the same way. Crew use the two <i><a href="/wiki/Strela_(crane)" title="Strela (crane)">Strela</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru" xml:lang="ru">\xd0\xa1\xd1\x82\xd1\x80\xd0\xb5\xd0\xbb\xd0\xb0\xcc\x81</span>; lit. Arrow) cargo cranes during EVAs for moving crew and equipment around the ROS. Each Strela crane has a mass of 45&#160;kg (99&#160;lb).</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Station_systems">Station systems</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_support">Life support</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/ISS_ECLSS" title="ISS ECLSS">ISS ECLSS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oxygen_generator" title="Chemical oxygen generator">Chemical oxygen generator</a></div>\n<p>The critical systems are the atmosphere control system, the water supply system, the food supply facilities, the sanitation and hygiene equipment, and fire detection and suppression equipment. The Russian Orbital Segment\'s life support systems are contained in the <i>Zvezda</i> service module. Some of these systems are supplemented by equipment in the USOS. The MLM <i>Nauka</i> laboratory has a complete set of life support systems.</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmospheric_control_systems">Atmospheric control systems</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SpaceStationCycle.svg" class="image"><img alt="A flowchart diagram showing the components of the ISS life support system." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SpaceStationCycle.svg/220px-SpaceStationCycle.svg.png" width="220" height="132" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SpaceStationCycle.svg/330px-SpaceStationCycle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/SpaceStationCycle.svg/440px-SpaceStationCycle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="705" data-file-height="422" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SpaceStationCycle.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe interactions between the components of the ISS Environmental Control and Life Support System (ECLSS)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The atmosphere on board the ISS is similar to the <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Atmosphere of Earth">Earth\'s</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> Normal air pressure on the ISS is 101.3&#160;<a href="/wiki/Kilopascal" class="mw-redirect" title="Kilopascal">kPa</a> (14.7&#160;<a href="/wiki/Pounds_per_square_inch" title="Pounds per square inch">psi</a>);<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> the same as at sea level on Earth. An Earth-like atmosphere offers benefits for crew comfort, and is much safer than the alternative, a pure oxygen atmosphere, because of the increased risk of a fire such as that responsible for the deaths of the <a href="/wiki/Apollo_1" title="Apollo 1">Apollo 1</a> crew.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Earth-like atmospheric conditions have been maintained on all Russian and Soviet spacecraft.<sup id="cite_ref-spacemed_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spacemed-163">[163]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Elektron_(ISS)" class="mw-redirect" title="Elektron (ISS)">Elektron</a></i> system aboard <i>Zvezda</i> and a similar system in <i>Destiny</i> generate oxygen aboard the station.<sup id="cite_ref-OGS_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OGS-164">[164]</a></sup> The crew has a backup option in the form of bottled oxygen and <a href="/wiki/Vika_oxygen_generator" title="Vika oxygen generator">Solid Fuel Oxygen Generation</a> (SFOG) canisters, a <a href="/wiki/Chemical_oxygen_generator" title="Chemical oxygen generator">chemical oxygen generator</a> system.<sup id="cite_ref-breath_easy_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breath_easy-165">[165]</a></sup> Carbon dioxide is removed from the air by the <a href="/wiki/Vozdukh" class="mw-redirect" title="Vozdukh">Vozdukh</a> system in <i>Zvezda</i>. Other by-products of human metabolism, such as methane from the intestines and ammonia from sweat, are removed by <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated charcoal</a> filters.<sup id="cite_ref-breath_easy_165-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-breath_easy-165">[165]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Part of the ROS atmosphere control system is the oxygen supply. Triple-redundancy is provided by the Elektron unit, solid fuel generators, and stored oxygen. The primary supply of oxygen is the Elektron unit which produces O<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />\n2</span> and H<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />\n2</span> by <a href="/wiki/Electrolysis" title="Electrolysis">electrolysis</a> of water and vents <span class="chemf nowrap">H2</span> overboard. The 1&#160;kW system uses approximately one litre of water per crew member per day. This water is either brought from Earth or recycled from other systems. <i>Mir</i> was the first spacecraft to use recycled water for oxygen production. The secondary oxygen supply is provided by burning O<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />\n2</span>-producing <a href="/wiki/Vika_oxygen_generator" title="Vika oxygen generator">Vika</a> cartridges (see also <a href="/wiki/ISS_ECLSS" title="ISS ECLSS">ISS ECLSS</a>). Each \'candle\' takes 5\xe2\x80\x9320 minutes to decompose at 450\xe2\x80\x93500&#160;\xc2\xb0C, producing 600 litres of O<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />\n2</span>. This unit is manually operated.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[166]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The US Orbital Segment has redundant supplies of oxygen, from a pressurised storage tank on the <i>Quest</i> airlock module delivered in 2001, supplemented ten years later by ESA-built Advanced Closed-Loop System (ACLS) in the <i>Tranquility</i> module (Node 3), which produces O<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />\n2</span> by electrolysis.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> Hydrogen produced is combined with carbon dioxide from the cabin atmosphere and converted to water and methane.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Power_and_thermal_control">Power and thermal control</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Electrical_system_of_the_International_Space_Station" title="Electrical system of the International Space Station">Electrical system of the International Space Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/External_Active_Thermal_Control_System" title="External Active Thermal Control System">External Active Thermal Control System</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:408px;max-width:408px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:ROSSA.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ROSSA.jpg/200px-ROSSA.jpg" width="200" height="133" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ROSSA.jpg/300px-ROSSA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/ROSSA.jpg/400px-ROSSA.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3060" data-file-height="2032" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Russian solar arrays, backlit by sunset.</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:P4_deployed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/P4_deployed.jpg/200px-P4_deployed.jpg" width="200" height="132" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/P4_deployed.jpg/300px-P4_deployed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/P4_deployed.jpg/400px-P4_deployed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2000" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">One of the eight truss mounted pairs of USOS solar arrays</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Double-sided solar, or <a href="/wiki/Photovoltaic" class="mw-redirect" title="Photovoltaic">Photovoltaic</a>, arrays provide <a href="/wiki/Electric_power" title="Electric power">electrical power</a> for the ISS. These bifacial cells are more efficient and operate at a lower temperature than single-sided cells commonly used on Earth, by collecting sunlight on one side and light <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">reflected off</a> the Earth on the other.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The Russian segment of the station, like the Space Shuttle and most spacecraft, uses 28&#160;<a href="/wiki/Volt" title="Volt">volt</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Direct_current" title="Direct current">DC</a> from four rotating solar arrays mounted on <i>Zarya</i> and <i>Zvezda</i>. The USOS uses 130\xe2\x80\x93180&#160;V&#160;DC from the USOS&#160;PV array, power is stabilised and distributed at 160&#160;V&#160;DC and converted to the user-required 124&#160;V&#160;DC. The <a href="/wiki/High_voltage" title="High voltage">higher distribution voltage</a> allows smaller, lighter conductors, at the expense of crew safety. The ROS uses <a href="/wiki/Extra-low_voltage" title="Extra-low voltage">low voltage</a>; the two station segments share power with converters.</p>\n<p>The USOS solar arrays are arranged as four wing pairs, for a total production of 75 to 90 kilowatts.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup> These arrays normally track the sun to maximise power generation. Each array is about 375&#160;m<sup>2</sup> (4,036&#160;sq&#160;ft) in area and 58&#160;m (190&#160;ft) long. In the complete configuration, the solar arrays track the sun by rotating the <i>alpha <a href="/wiki/Gimbal" title="Gimbal">gimbal</a></i> once per orbit; the <i>beta gimbal</i> follows slower changes in the angle of the sun to the orbital plane. The <a href="/wiki/Night_Glider_mode" title="Night Glider mode">Night Glider mode</a> aligns the solar arrays parallel to the ground at night to reduce the significant aerodynamic drag at the station\'s relatively low orbital altitude.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The station uses rechargeable <a href="/wiki/Nickel%E2%80%93hydrogen_battery" title="Nickel\xe2\x80\x93hydrogen battery">nickel\xe2\x80\x93hydrogen batteries</a> (NiH<span style="display:inline-block; margin-bottom:-0.3em; vertical-align:-0.4em; line-height:1.2em; font-size:70%; text-align:left;"><br />\n2</span>) for continuous power during the 35 minutes of every 90-minute orbit that it is eclipsed by the Earth. The batteries are recharged on the day side of the Earth. They have a 6.5-year lifetime (over 37,000 charge/discharge cycles) and will be regularly replaced over the anticipated 20-year life of the station.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> As of 2017, the nickel\xe2\x80\x93hydrogen batteries are being replaced by <a href="/wiki/Lithium-ion_battery" title="Lithium-ion battery">lithium-ion batteries</a>, which are expected to last until the end of the ISS program.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The station\'s large solar panels generate a high potential voltage difference between the station and the ionosphere. This could cause arcing through insulating surfaces and sputtering of conductive surfaces as ions are accelerated by the spacecraft plasma sheath. To mitigate this, plasma contactor units (PCU)s create current paths between the station and the ambient plasma field.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:EATCS.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/EATCS.png/220px-EATCS.png" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/EATCS.png/330px-EATCS.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/EATCS.png/440px-EATCS.png 2x" data-file-width="786" data-file-height="493" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:EATCS.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nISS External Active Thermal Control System (EATCS) diagram</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The station\'s systems and experiments consume a large amount of electrical power, almost all of which converts to heat. Little of this heat dissipates through the walls of the station. To keep the internal ambient temperature within comfortable, workable limits, <a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">ammonia</a> is continuously pumped through pipes throughout the station to collect heat, then into external radiators to emit infrared radiation, then back into the station.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">[174]</a></sup> Thus this passive thermal control system (PTCS) is made of external surface materials, insulation such as MLI, and heat pipes.</p>\n<p>If the PTCS cannot keep up with the heat load, an External Active Thermal Control System (EATCS) maintains the temperature. The EATCS consists of an internal, non-toxic, water coolant loop used to cool and dehumidify the atmosphere, which transfers collected heat into an external liquid ammonia loop that can withstand the much lower temperature of space, and is circulated through radiators to remove the heat. The EATCS provides cooling for all the US pressurised modules, including <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> and <i>Columbus</i>, as well as the main power distribution electronics of the S0, S1 and P1 trusses. It can reject up to 70&#160;kW. This is much more than the 14&#160;kW of the Early External Active Thermal Control System (EEATCS) via the Early Ammonia Servicer (EAS), which was launched on <a href="/wiki/STS-105" title="STS-105">STS-105</a> and installed onto the P6 Truss.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Communications_and_computers">Communications and computers</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Tracking_and_Data_Relay_Satellite" class="mw-redirect" title="Tracking and Data Relay Satellite">Tracking and Data Relay Satellite</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luch_(satellite)" title="Luch (satellite)">Luch (satellite)</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/ThinkPad#Use_in_space" title="ThinkPad">ThinkPad \xc2\xa7&#160;Use in space</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Communication_Systems.png" class="image"><img alt="Diagram showing communications links between the ISS and other elements." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/ISS_Communication_Systems.png/220px-ISS_Communication_Systems.png" width="220" height="260" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/ISS_Communication_Systems.png/330px-ISS_Communication_Systems.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/ISS_Communication_Systems.png/440px-ISS_Communication_Systems.png 2x" data-file-width="1402" data-file-height="1658" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Communication_Systems.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe communications systems used by the ISS<br />\n* Luch satellite and the Space Shuttle are not currently in use</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Radio communications provide <a href="/wiki/Telemetry" title="Telemetry">telemetry</a> and scientific data links between the station and <a href="/wiki/Mission_Control_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Mission Control Center">Mission Control Centres</a>. Radio links are also used during <a href="/wiki/Space_rendezvous" title="Space rendezvous">rendezvous and docking procedures</a> and for audio and video communication between crew members, flight controllers and family members. As a result, the ISS is equipped with internal and external communication systems used for different purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-BoeingComm_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoeingComm-176">[176]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The Russian Orbital Segment communicates directly with the ground via the <i><a href="/wiki/Lira_(ISS)" title="Lira (ISS)">Lira</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Antenna_(radio)" title="Antenna (radio)">antenna</a> mounted to <i>Zvezda</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[177]</a></sup> The <i>Lira</i> antenna also has the capability to use the <i><a href="/wiki/Luch_(satellite)" title="Luch (satellite)">Luch</a></i> data relay satellite system.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup> This system, used for communications with <i>Mir</i>, fell into disrepair during the 1990s, and so is no longer in use,<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-SSSM_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SSSM-178">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Harvey_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harvey-179">[179]</a></sup> although two new <i>Luch</i> satellites\xe2\x80\x94<i>Luch</i>-5A and <i>Luch</i>-5B\xe2\x80\x94were launched in 2011 and 2012 respectively to restore the operational capability of the system.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> Another Russian communications system is the <a href="/wiki/Voskhod-M" title="Voskhod-M">Voskhod-M</a>, which enables internal telephone communications between <i>Zvezda</i>, <i>Zarya</i>, <i>Pirs</i>, <i>Poisk</i> and the USOS, and also provides a VHF radio link to ground control centres via antennas on <i>Zvezda</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> exterior.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/US_Orbital_Segment" title="US Orbital Segment">US Orbital Segment</a> (USOS) makes use of two separate radio links mounted in the <a href="/wiki/Z1_truss" class="mw-redirect" title="Z1 truss">Z1 truss</a> structure: the <a href="/wiki/S_band" title="S band">S band</a> (used for audio) and <a href="/wiki/Ku_band" title="Ku band">K<sub>u</sub> band</a> (used for audio, video and data) systems. These transmissions are routed via the United States <a href="/wiki/Tracking_and_Data_Relay_Satellite" class="mw-redirect" title="Tracking and Data Relay Satellite">Tracking and Data Relay Satellite</a> System (TDRSS) in <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">geostationary orbit</a>, which allows for almost continuous real-time communications with <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">NASA\'s Mission Control Center</a> (MCC-H) in <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSBook_10-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSBook-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BoeingComm_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BoeingComm-176">[176]</a></sup> Data channels for the Canadarm2, European <i>Columbus</i> laboratory and Japanese <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> modules are routed via the S band and K<sub>u</sub> band systems, although the <a href="/wiki/European_Data_Relay_System" title="European Data Relay System">European Data Relay System</a> and a similar Japanese system will eventually complement the TDRSS in this role.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSBook_10-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSBook-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-JAXA-MOU_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JAXA-MOU-182">[182]</a></sup> Communications between modules are carried on an internal digital <a href="/wiki/Wireless_network" title="Wireless network">wireless network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg/220px-STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg/330px-STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg/440px-STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2040" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-128_ISS-20_Destiny_Canadarm2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nLaptop computers surround the Canadarm2 console.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Ultra_high_frequency" title="Ultra high frequency">UHF radio</a> is used by astronauts and cosmonauts conducting <a href="/wiki/Extra-vehicular_activity" class="mw-redirect" title="Extra-vehicular activity">EVAs</a>. UHF is used by other spacecraft that dock to or undock from the station, such as Soyuz, Progress, HTV, ATV and the Space Shuttle (except the shuttle also makes use of the S band and K<sub>u</sub> band systems via TDRSS), to receive commands from Mission Control and ISS crewmembers.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup> Automated spacecraft are fitted with their own communications equipment; the ATV uses a <a href="/wiki/Laser" title="Laser">laser</a> attached to the spacecraft and equipment attached to <i>Zvezda</i>, known as the Proximity Communications Equipment, to accurately dock to the station.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The ISS is equipped with approximately 100 <a href="/wiki/IBM" title="IBM">IBM</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lenovo" title="Lenovo">Lenovo</a> <a href="/wiki/ThinkPad#Use_in_space" title="ThinkPad">ThinkPad</a> model A31 and T61P laptop computers. Each computer is a <a href="/wiki/Commercial_off-the-shelf" title="Commercial off-the-shelf">commercial off-the-shelf</a> purchase which is then modified for safety and operation including updates to connectors, cooling and power to accommodate the station\'s 28V DC power system and weightless environment. Heat generated by the laptops does not rise but stagnates around the laptop, so additional forced ventilation is required. Laptops aboard the ISS are connected to the station\'s <a href="/wiki/Wireless_LAN" title="Wireless LAN">wireless LAN</a> via <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> and to the ground via K<sub>u</sub> band. This provides speeds of 10&#160;<a href="/wiki/Megabit_per_second" class="mw-redirect" title="Megabit per second">Mbit/s</a> to and 3&#160;Mbit/s from the station, comparable to home <a href="/wiki/Digital_subscriber_line" title="Digital subscriber line">DSL</a> connection speeds.<sup id="cite_ref-issit_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-issit-186">[186]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tested20121019_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tested20121019-187">[187]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The operating system used for key station functions is the <a href="/wiki/Debian" title="Debian">Debian</a> <a href="/wiki/Linux_distribution" title="Linux distribution">Linux distribution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> The migration from <a href="/wiki/Microsoft_Windows" title="Microsoft Windows">Microsoft Windows</a> was made in May 2013 for reasons of reliability, stability and flexibility.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Station_operations">Station operations</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Expeditions_and_private_flights">Expeditions and private flights</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sts088-703-019e.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sts088-703-019e.jpg/220px-Sts088-703-019e.jpg" width="220" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sts088-703-019e.jpg/330px-Sts088-703-019e.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sts088-703-019e.jpg/440px-Sts088-703-019e.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1716" data-file-height="1448" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sts088-703-019e.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<i>Zarya</i> and <i>Unity</i> were entered for the first time on 10 December 1998.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg/220px-Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg/330px-Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg/440px-Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="1968" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Soyuz_tm-31_transported_to_launch_pad.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSoyuz TM-31 being prepared to bring the first resident crew to the station in October 2000</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-115_ISS_after_undocking.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/STS-115_ISS_after_undocking.jpg/220px-STS-115_ISS_after_undocking.jpg" width="220" height="138" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/STS-115_ISS_after_undocking.jpg/330px-STS-115_ISS_after_undocking.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/STS-115_ISS_after_undocking.jpg/440px-STS-115_ISS_after_undocking.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2944" data-file-height="1840" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-115_ISS_after_undocking.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nISS was slowly assembled over a decade of spaceflights and crews</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg/220px-Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg/330px-Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg/440px-Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Tracy_Caldwell_Dyson_in_Cupola_ISS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nExpeditions have included crew members from many nations</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><i>See also the <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_expeditions" title="List of International Space Station expeditions">list of International Space Station expeditions</a> (professional crew), <a href="/wiki/Space_tourism" title="Space tourism">space tourism</a> (private travellers), and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_spaceflights_to_the_ISS" class="mw-redirect" title="List of human spaceflights to the ISS">list of human spaceflights to the ISS</a> (both).</i></p>\n<p>Each permanent crew is given an expedition number. Expeditions run up to six months, from launch until undocking, an \'increment\' covers the same time period, but includes cargo ships and all activities. Expeditions 1 to 6 consisted of 3 person crews, Expeditions 7 to 12 were reduced to the safe minimum of two following the destruction of the NASA Shuttle Columbia. From Expedition 13 the crew gradually increased to 6 around 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSEx_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSEx-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-current_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-current-191">[191]</a></sup> With the arrival of the American <a href="/wiki/Commercial_Crew_Development" title="Commercial Crew Development">Commercial Crew</a> vehicles in the middle of the 2010s, expedition size may be increased to seven crew members, the number ISS is designed for.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Sergei_Krikalev" title="Sergei Krikalev">Sergei Krikalev</a>, member of <a href="/wiki/Expedition_1" title="Expedition 1">Expedition 1</a> and Commander of <a href="/wiki/Expedition_11" title="Expedition 11">Expedition 11</a>, has spent more time in space than anyone else, a total of 803 days and 9 hours and 39 minutes. His awards include the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Lenin" title="Order of Lenin">Order of Lenin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="Hero of the Soviet Union">Hero of the Soviet Union</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hero_of_the_Russian_Federation" title="Hero of the Russian Federation">Hero of the Russian Federation</a>, and 4 NASA medals. On 16 August 2005 at 1:44&#160;am EDT, he passed the record of 748 days held by <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Avdeyev" title="Sergei Avdeyev">Sergei Avdeyev</a>, who had \'time travelled\' 1/50th of a second into the future aboard <i><a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> He participated in psychosocial experiment SFINCSS-99 (Simulation of Flight of International Crew on Space Station), which examined inter-cultural and other stress factors affecting integration of crew in preparation for the ISS spaceflights. <a href="/wiki/Scott_Kelly_(astronaut)" title="Scott Kelly (astronaut)">Scott Kelly</a> has spent the most time in space of any American. Kelly returned from the ISS on 1 March 2016 having spent 340 consecutive days in orbit.</p>\n<p>Travellers who pay for their own passage into space are termed spaceflight participants by Roscosmos and NASA, and are sometimes referred to as space tourists, a term they generally dislike.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[note 1]</a></sup> All seven were transported to the ISS on Russian Soyuz spacecraft. When professional crews change over in numbers not divisible by the three seats in a Soyuz, and a short-stay crewmember is not sent, the spare seat is sold by MirCorp through Space Adventures. When the space shuttle retired in 2011, and the station\'s crew size was reduced to 6, space tourism was halted, as the partners relied on Russian transport seats for access to the station. Soyuz flight schedules increase after 2013, allowing 5 Soyuz flights (15 seats) with only two expeditions (12 seats) required.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup> The remaining seats are sold for around <span style="white-space: nowrap">US$40 million</span> to members of the public who can pass a medical exam. ESA and NASA criticised private spaceflight at the beginning of the ISS, and NASA initially resisted training <a href="/wiki/Dennis_Tito" title="Dennis Tito">Dennis Tito</a>, the first man to pay for his own passage to the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[note 2]</a></sup></p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Anousheh_Ansari" title="Anousheh Ansari">Anousheh Ansari</a> became the first Iranian in space and the first self-funded woman to fly to the station. Officials reported that her education and experience make her much more than a tourist, and her performance in training had been "excellent."<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[201]</a></sup> Ansari herself dismisses the idea that she is a tourist. She did Russian and European studies involving medicine and microbiology during her 10-day stay. The documentary <i><a href="/wiki/Space_Tourists" title="Space Tourists">Space Tourists</a></i> follows her journey to the station, where she fulfilled "an age-old dream of man: to leave our planet as a "normal person" and travel into outer space."<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[202]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In 2008, spaceflight participant <a href="/wiki/Richard_Garriott" title="Richard Garriott">Richard Garriott</a> placed a <a href="/wiki/Geocaching" title="Geocaching">geocache</a> aboard the ISS during his flight.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[203]</a></sup> This is currently the only non-terrestrial geocache in existence.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[204]</a></sup> At the same time, the <a href="/wiki/Immortality_Drive" title="Immortality Drive">Immortality Drive</a>, an electronic record of eight digitised human DNA sequences, was placed aboard the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[205]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit">Orbit</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:543px;max-width:543px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Internationale_Raumstation_Bahnh%C3%B6he_(dumb_version).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Internationale_Raumstation_Bahnh%C3%B6he_%28dumb_version%29.png/300px-Internationale_Raumstation_Bahnh%C3%B6he_%28dumb_version%29.png" width="300" height="186" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Internationale_Raumstation_Bahnh%C3%B6he_%28dumb_version%29.png/450px-Internationale_Raumstation_Bahnh%C3%B6he_%28dumb_version%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Internationale_Raumstation_Bahnh%C3%B6he_%28dumb_version%29.png/600px-Internationale_Raumstation_Bahnh%C3%B6he_%28dumb_version%29.png 2x" data-file-width="970" data-file-height="602" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Graph showing the changing altitude of the ISS from November 1998 until January 2009</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:237px;max-width:237px">\n<div class="thumbimage">\n<div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:235px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;width:360px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; poster=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Iss.ogv/360px--Iss.ogv.jpg&quot; controls=&quot;&quot; preload=&quot;none&quot; autoplay=&quot;&quot;
