data-mwprovider=&quot;wikimediacommons&quot;&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Iss.ogv&quot; type=&quot;video/ogg; codecs=&amp;quot;theora&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Original Ogg file, 360 \xc3\x97 240 (211 kbps)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;Ogg source&quot; data-width=&quot;360&quot; data-height=&quot;240&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;210613&quot; data-framerate=&quot;30&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/60/Iss.ogv/Iss.ogv.240p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Small WebM (240P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 240P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;240p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;360&quot; data-height=&quot;240&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;247024&quot; data-framerate=&quot;30&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/6/60/Iss.ogv/Iss.ogv.160p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth WebM (160P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 160P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;160p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;240&quot; data-height=&quot;160&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;124208&quot; data-framerate=&quot;30&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Iss.ogv" style="width:235px;height:157px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Iss.ogv/235px--Iss.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Iss.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Animation of ISS orbit from a North American geostationary point of view (sped up 1800 times)</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The ISS is maintained in a nearly circular orbit with a minimum mean altitude of 330&#160;km (205&#160;mi) and a maximum of 410&#160;km (255&#160;mi), in the centre of the <a href="/wiki/Thermosphere" title="Thermosphere">thermosphere</a>, at an <a href="/wiki/Inclination" class="mw-redirect" title="Inclination">inclination</a> of 51.6 degrees to Earth\'s equator, necessary to ensure that Russian <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_(spacecraft)" title="Soyuz (spacecraft)">Soyuz</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft)" title="Progress (spacecraft)">Progress</a> spacecraft launched from the <a href="/wiki/Baikonur_Cosmodrome" title="Baikonur Cosmodrome">Baikonur Cosmodrome</a> may be safely launched to reach the station. Spent rocket stages must be dropped into uninhabited areas and this limits the directions rockets can be launched from the spaceport.<sup id="cite_ref-MCC_Answer_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCC_Answer-208">[206]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[207]</a></sup> It travels at an average speed of 27,724 kilometres per hour (17,227&#160;mph), and completes 15.54&#160;orbits per day (93 minutes per orbit).<sup id="cite_ref-heavens-above_3-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-heavens-above-3">[3]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tracking_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tracking-15">[15]</a></sup> The station\'s altitude was allowed to fall around the time of each NASA shuttle mission. Orbital boost burns would generally be delayed until after the shuttle\'s departure. This allowed shuttle payloads to be lifted with the station\'s engines during the routine firings, rather than have the shuttle lift itself and the payload together to a higher orbit. This trade-off allowed heavier loads to be transferred to the station. After the retirement of the NASA shuttle, the nominal orbit of the space station was raised in altitude.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[208]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Popular_Mechanics_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Popular_Mechanics-211">[209]</a></sup> Other, more frequent supply ships do not require this adjustment as they are substantially lighter vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-Worldbook_at_NASA_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Worldbook_at_NASA-32">[32]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-nasa.gov-iss-environment_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov-iss-environment-212">[210]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg/220px-ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg/330px-ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg/440px-ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1010" data-file-height="653" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_orbits_04132013.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nOrbits of the ISS, shown in April 2013</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Orbital boosting can be performed by the station\'s two main engines on the <i><a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)">Zvezda</a></i> service module, or Russian or European spacecraft docked to <i>Zvezda</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> aft port. The ATV has been designed with the possibility of adding a <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle#ATV_evolution_proposals" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">second docking port</a> to its other end, allowing it to remain at the ISS and still allow other craft to dock and boost the station. It takes approximately two orbits (three hours) for the boost to a higher altitude to be completed.<sup id="cite_ref-nasa.gov-iss-environment_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nasa.gov-iss-environment-212">[210]</a></sup> Maintaining ISS altitude uses about 7.5 tonnes of chemical fuel per annum<sup id="cite_ref-newscientist.com_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newscientist.com-213">[211]</a></sup> at an annual cost of about $210 million.<sup id="cite_ref-aaESummary20100124_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-aaESummary20100124-214">[212]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In December 2008 NASA signed an agreement with the <a href="/wiki/Ad_Astra_Rocket_Company" title="Ad Astra Rocket Company">Ad Astra Rocket Company</a> which may result in the testing on the ISS of a <a href="/wiki/VASIMR" class="mw-redirect" title="VASIMR">VASIMR</a> plasma propulsion engine.<sup id="cite_ref-vasimr_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vasimr-215">[213]</a></sup> This technology could allow <a href="/wiki/Orbital_station-keeping" title="Orbital station-keeping">station-keeping</a> to be done more economically than at present.<sup id="cite_ref-future-prop_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-future-prop-216">[214]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[215]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The Russian Orbital Segment contains the Data Management System, which handles Guidance, Navigation and Control (ROS GNC) for the entire station.<sup id="cite_ref-Navigation_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Navigation-95">[95]</a></sup> Initially, <i>Zarya</i>, the first module of the station, controlled the station until a short time after the Russian service module <i>Zvezda</i> docked and was transferred control. <i>Zvezda</i> contains the ESA built DMS-R Data Management System.<sup id="cite_ref-EsaComputer_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EsaComputer-218">[216]</a></sup> Using two fault-tolerant computers (FTC), <i>Zvezda</i> computes the station\'s position and orbital trajectory using redundant Earth horizon sensors, Solar horizon sensors as well as Sun and star trackers. The FTCs each contain three identical processing units working in parallel and provide advanced fault-masking by majority voting.</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Orientation">Orientation</span></h4>\n<p><i>Zvezda</i> uses gyroscopes and thrusters to turn itself around. Gyroscopes do not require propellant, rather they use electricity to \'store\' momentum in flywheels by turning in the opposite direction to the station\'s movement. The USOS has its own computer controlled gyroscopes to handle the extra mass of that section. When gyroscopes <a href="/wiki/Control_moment_gyroscope#Saturation" title="Control moment gyroscope">\'saturate\'</a>, thrusters are used to cancel out the stored momentum. During <a href="/wiki/Expedition_10" title="Expedition 10">Expedition 10</a>, an incorrect command was sent to the station\'s computer, using about 14 kilograms of propellant before the fault was noticed and fixed. When attitude control computers in the ROS and USOS fail to communicate properly, it can result in a rare \'force fight\' where the ROS GNC computer must ignore the USOS counterpart, which has no thrusters.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[218]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[219]</a></sup> When an ATV, NASA Shuttle, or Soyuz is docked to the station, it can also be used to maintain station attitude such as for troubleshooting. Shuttle control was used exclusively during <a href="/wiki/STS-117" title="STS-117">installation</a> of the S3/S4 truss, which provides electrical power and data interfaces for the station\'s electronics.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[220]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mission_controls">Mission controls</span></h2>\n<p>The components of the ISS are operated and monitored by their respective space agencies at <a href="/wiki/Mission_Control_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Mission Control Center">mission control centres</a> across the globe, including:</p>\n<ul>\n<li>Roscosmos\'s <a href="/wiki/Mission_Control_Center#RKA_Mission_Control_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Mission Control Center">Mission Control Center</a> at <a href="/wiki/Korolyov_(city)" class="mw-redirect" title="Korolyov (city)">Korolyov</a>, Moscow Oblast, controls the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment" title="Russian Orbital Segment">Russian Orbital Segment</a> which handles Guidance, Navigation &amp; Control for the entire Station.,<sup id="cite_ref-Navigation_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Navigation-95">[95]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EsaComputer_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EsaComputer-218">[216]</a></sup> in addition to individual Soyuz and Progress missions.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup></li>\n<li>ESA\'s <a href="/wiki/ATV_Control_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="ATV Control Centre">ATV Control Centre</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Toulouse_Space_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Toulouse Space Centre">Toulouse Space Centre</a> (CST) in <a href="/wiki/Toulouse" title="Toulouse">Toulouse</a>, France, controls flights of the unmanned European <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">Automated Transfer Vehicle</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup></li>\n<li>JAXA\'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=JEM_Control_Center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="JEM Control Center (page does not exist)">JEM Control Center</a> and <a href="/w/index.php?title=HTV_Control_Center&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="HTV Control Center (page does not exist)">HTV Control Center</a> at <a href="/wiki/Tsukuba_Space_Center" title="Tsukuba Space Center">Tsukuba Space Center</a> (TKSC) in <a href="/wiki/Tsukuba" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsukuba">Tsukuba</a>, Japan, are responsible for operating the <i>Kib\xc5\x8d</i> complex and all flights of the \'White Stork\' HTV Cargo spacecraft, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup></li>\n<li>NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Christopher_C._Kraft_Jr._Mission_Control_Center" title="Christopher C. Kraft Jr. Mission Control Center">Mission Control Center</a> at <a href="/wiki/Lyndon_B._Johnson_Space_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center">Lyndon B. Johnson Space Center</a> in Houston, Texas, serves as the primary control facility for the United States segment of the ISS and also controlled the Space Shuttle missions that visited the station.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup></li>\n<li>NASA\'s <a href="/wiki/Payload_Operations_and_Integration_Center" title="Payload Operations and Integration Center">Payload Operations and Integration Center</a> at <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Space_Flight_Center" title="Marshall Space Flight Center">Marshall Space Flight Center</a> in <a href="/wiki/Huntsville,_Alabama" title="Huntsville, Alabama">Huntsville, Alabama</a>, coordinates payload operations in the USOS.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup></li>\n<li>ESA\'s <a href="/wiki/Columbus_Control_Center" class="mw-redirect" title="Columbus Control Center"><i>Columbus</i> Control Centre</a> at the <a href="/wiki/German_Aerospace_Center" title="German Aerospace Center">German Aerospace Centre</a> (DLR) in <a href="/wiki/Oberpfaffenhofen" title="Oberpfaffenhofen">Oberpfaffenhofen</a>, Germany, manages the European <i>Columbus</i> research laboratory.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup></li>\n<li>CSA\'s <a href="/w/index.php?title=MSS_Control&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="MSS Control (page does not exist)">MSS Control</a> at <a href="/wiki/Saint-Hubert,_Quebec" title="Saint-Hubert, Quebec">Saint-Hubert, Quebec</a>, Canada, controls and monitors the <a href="/wiki/Mobile_Servicing_System" title="Mobile Servicing System">Mobile Servicing System</a>, or Canadarm2.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSRG_16-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSRG-16">[16]</a></sup></li>\n</ul>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; overflow:hidden; width:auto; max-width:888px;">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="">\n<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow:auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Centers.svg" class="image" title="Space centres involved with the ISS programme"><img alt="A world map highlighting the locations of space centres. See adjacent text for details." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/ISS_Centers.svg/880px-ISS_Centers.svg.png" width="880" height="260" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/ISS_Centers.svg/1320px-ISS_Centers.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/ISS_Centers.svg/1760px-ISS_Centers.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="3388" data-file-height="1000" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Centers.svg" title="File:ISS Centers.svg"></a></div>\nSpace centres involved with the ISS programme</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Repairs">Repairs</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Replacement_Units" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbital Replacement Units">Orbital Replacement Units</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station_maintenance" title="International Space Station maintenance">International Space Station maintenance</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png/220px-ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png" width="220" height="98" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png/330px-ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png/440px-ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png 2x" data-file-width="1112" data-file-height="493" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_Unpressurized_Platforms.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSpare parts are called <a href="/wiki/Orbit_Replaceable_Unit" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbit Replaceable Unit">ORUs</a>; some are externally stored on pallets called <a href="/wiki/ExPRESS_Logistics_Carrier" title="ExPRESS Logistics Carrier">ELCs</a> and <a href="/wiki/External_stowage_platform" title="External stowage platform">ESPs</a>.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><b><a href="/wiki/Orbital_Replacement_Units" class="mw-redirect" title="Orbital Replacement Units">Orbital Replacement Units</a></b> (<b>ORUs</b>) are spare parts that can be readily replaced when a unit either passes its design life or fails. Examples of ORUs are pumps, storage tanks, controller boxes, antennas, and battery units. Some units can be replaced using robotic arms. Many are stored outside the station, either on small pallets called <a href="/wiki/ExPRESS_Logistics_Carrier" title="ExPRESS Logistics Carrier">ExPRESS Logistics Carriers</a> (ELCs) or share larger platforms called <a href="/wiki/External_Stowage_Platform" class="mw-redirect" title="External Stowage Platform">External Stowage Platforms</a> which also hold science experiments. Both kinds of pallets have electricity as many parts which could be damaged by the cold of space require heating. The larger logistics carriers also have computer local area network connections (LAN) and telemetry to connect experiments. A heavy emphasis on stocking the USOS with ORU\'s occurred around 2011, before the end of the NASA shuttle programme, as its commercial replacements, <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft)" title="Cygnus (spacecraft)">Cygnus</a> and <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon">Dragon</a>, carry one tenth to one quarter the payload.</p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Two black and orange solar arrays, shown uneven and with a large tear visible. A crew member in a spacesuit, attached to the end of a robotic arm, holds a latticework between two solar sails." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg/220px-STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg/330px-STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg/440px-STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3032" data-file-height="2008" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:STS-120_EVA_Scott_Parazynski.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nWhile anchored on the end of the <a href="/wiki/Orbiter_Boom_Sensor_System" title="Orbiter Boom Sensor System">OBSS</a> during <a href="/wiki/STS-120" title="STS-120">STS-120</a>, astronaut <a href="/wiki/Scott_Parazynski" class="mw-redirect" title="Scott Parazynski">Scott Parazynski</a> performs makeshift repairs to a US solar array which damaged itself when unfolding.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg/220px-Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg/330px-Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg/440px-Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2848" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Astronaut_Mike_Hopkins_on_Dec._24_Spacewalk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMike Hopkins on his Christmas Eve spacewalk</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Unexpected problems and failures have impacted the station\'s assembly time-line and work schedules leading to periods of reduced capabilities and, in some cases, could have forced abandonment of the station for safety reasons, had these problems not been resolved. During <a href="/wiki/STS-120" title="STS-120">STS-120</a> in 2007, following the relocation of the P6 truss and solar arrays, it was noted during the redeployment of the array that it had become torn and was not deploying properly.<sup id="cite_ref-Astronauts_notice_tear_in_solar_panel_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Astronauts_notice_tear_in_solar_panel-223">[221]</a></sup> An EVA was carried out by <a href="/wiki/Scott_E._Parazynski" title="Scott E. Parazynski">Scott Parazynski</a>, assisted by <a href="/wiki/Douglas_H._Wheelock" title="Douglas H. Wheelock">Douglas Wheelock</a>. The men took extra precautions to reduce the risk of electric shock, as the repairs were carried out with the solar array exposed to sunlight.<sup id="cite_ref-Space_Station.27s_Damaged_Panel_Is_Fixed_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Space_Station.27s_Damaged_Panel_Is_Fixed-224">[222]</a></sup> The issues with the array were followed in the same year by problems with the starboard Solar Alpha Rotary Joint (SARJ), which rotates the arrays on the starboard side of the station. Excessive vibration and high-current spikes in the array drive motor were noted, resulting in a decision to substantially curtail motion of the starboard SARJ until the cause was understood. Inspections during EVAs on STS-120 and <a href="/wiki/STS-123" title="STS-123">STS-123</a> showed extensive contamination from metallic shavings and debris in the large drive gear and confirmed damage to the large metallic race ring at the heart of the joint, and so the joint was locked to prevent further damage.<sup id="cite_ref-joint-update_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-joint-update-225">[223]</a></sup> Repairs to the joint were carried out during <a href="/wiki/STS-126" title="STS-126">STS-126</a> with lubrication of both joints and the replacement of 11 out of 12 trundle bearings on the joint.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[224]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[225]</a></sup></p>\n<p>2009 saw damage to the S1 radiator, one of the components of the station\'s cooling system. The problem was first noticed in <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_spacecraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Soyuz spacecraft">Soyuz</a> imagery in September 2008, but was not thought to be serious.<sup id="cite_ref-Radiator_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radiator-228">[226]</a></sup> The imagery showed that the surface of one sub-panel has peeled back from the underlying central structure, possibly because of micro-meteoroid or debris impact. It is also known that a Service Module thruster cover, jettisoned during an EVA in 2008, had struck the S1 radiator, but its effect, if any, has not been determined. On 15 May 2009 the damaged radiator panel\'s ammonia tubing was mechanically shut off from the rest of the cooling system by the computer-controlled closure of a valve. The same valve was used immediately afterwards to vent the ammonia from the damaged panel, eliminating the possibility of an ammonia leak from the cooling system via the damaged panel.<sup id="cite_ref-Radiator_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Radiator-228">[226]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Early on 1 August 2010, a failure in cooling Loop A (starboard side), one of two external cooling loops, left the station with only half of its normal cooling capacity and zero redundancy in some systems.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[227]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[228]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[229]</a></sup> The problem appeared to be in the ammonia pump module that circulates the ammonia cooling fluid. Several subsystems, including two of the four CMGs, were shut down.</p>\n<p>Planned operations on the ISS were interrupted through a series of EVAs to address the cooling system issue. A first EVA on 7 August 2010, to replace the failed pump module, was not fully completed because of an ammonia leak in one of four quick-disconnects. A second EVA on 11 August successfully removed the failed pump module.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[230]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[231]</a></sup> A third EVA was required to restore Loop A to normal functionality.<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[232]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[233]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The USOS\'s cooling system is largely built by the American company <a href="/wiki/Boeing" title="Boeing">Boeing</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[234]</a></sup> which is also the manufacturer of the failed pump.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[235]</a></sup></p>\n<p>An air leak from the USOS in 2004,<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[236]</a></sup> the venting of fumes from an <i><a href="/wiki/Elektron_(ISS)" class="mw-redirect" title="Elektron (ISS)">Elektron</a></i> oxygen generator in 2006,<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[237]</a></sup> and the failure of the computers in the ROS in 2007 during <a href="/wiki/STS-117" title="STS-117">STS-117</a> left the station without thruster, <i>Elektron</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Vozdukh" class="mw-redirect" title="Vozdukh">Vozdukh</a></i> and other environmental control system operations, the root cause of which was found to be condensation inside the electrical connectors leading to a short-circuit.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>\n<p>The four Main Bus Switching Units (MBSUs, located in the S0 truss), control the routing of power from the four solar array wings to the rest of the ISS. In late 2011 MBSU-1, while still routing power correctly, ceased responding to commands or sending data confirming its health, and was scheduled to be swapped out at the next available EVA. In each MBSU, two power channels feed 160V DC from the arrays to two DC-to-DC power converters (DDCUs) that supply the 124V power used in the station. A spare MBSU was already on board, but 30 August 2012 EVA failed to be completed when a bolt being tightened to finish installation of the spare unit jammed before electrical connection was secured.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[238]</a></sup> The loss of MBSU-1 limits the station to 75% of its normal power capacity, requiring minor limitations in normal operations until the problem can be addressed.</p>\n<p>On 5 September 2012, in a second, 6&#160;hr, EVA to replace MBSU-1, astronauts Sunita Williams and Akihiko Hoshide successfully restored the ISS to 100% power.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[239]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On 24 December 2013, astronauts made a rare Christmas Eve space walk, installing a new ammonia pump for the station\'s cooling system. The faulty cooling system had failed earlier in the month, halting many of the station\'s science experiments. Astronauts had to brave a "mini blizzard" of ammonia while installing the new pump. It was only the second Christmas Eve spacewalk in NASA history.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[240]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Fleet_operations">Fleet operations</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_human_spaceflights_to_the_International_Space_Station" title="List of human spaceflights to the International Space Station">List of human spaceflights to the International Space Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_unmanned_spaceflights_to_the_International_Space_Station" class="mw-redirect" title="List of unmanned spaceflights to the International Space Station">List of unmanned spaceflights to the International Space Station</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Iss_orbit_and_expeditions.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Iss_orbit_and_expeditions.png/220px-Iss_orbit_and_expeditions.png" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" 2014</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dragon_and_Cygnus_docked_on_ISS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Dragon_and_Cygnus_docked_on_ISS.jpg/220px-Dragon_and_Cygnus_docked_on_ISS.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Dragon_and_Cygnus_docked_on_ISS.jpg/330px-Dragon_and_Cygnus_docked_on_ISS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Dragon_and_Cygnus_docked_on_ISS.jpg/440px-Dragon_and_Cygnus_docked_on_ISS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="940" data-file-height="650" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dragon_and_Cygnus_docked_on_ISS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nDragon and Cygnus cargo vessels were docked at the ISS together for the first time in April 2016.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>A wide variety of manned and unmanned spacecraft have supported the station\'s activities. More than 60 Progress spacecraft, including <a href="/wiki/Progress_M-MIM2" title="Progress M-MIM2">M-MIM2</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progress_M-SO1" title="Progress M-SO1">M-SO1</a> which installed modules, and more than 40 Soyuz spacecraft have flown to the ISS. 35 flights of the retired NASA Space Shuttle were made to the station.<sup id="cite_ref-ISStD_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISStD-4">[4]</a></sup> There have been five European <a href="/wiki/Automated_Transfer_Vehicle" title="Automated Transfer Vehicle">ATV</a>, five Japanese <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">HTV \'Kounotori\'</a>, eight <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon">SpaceX Dragon</a> and four Orbital ATK <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_(spacecraft)" title="Cygnus (spacecraft)">Cygnus</a> flights.</p>\n<h3><span id="Currently_docked/berthed"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Currently_docked.2Fberthed">Currently docked/berthed</span></h3>\n<p><i>See also the list of <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Space_Station_expeditions" title="List of International Space Station expeditions">professional crew</a>, <a spaceflights</a>.</i></p>\n<dl>\n<dt>Key</dt>\n</dl>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" missions</span></h3>\n<ul>\n<li>All dates are <a href="/wiki/UTC" class="mw-redirect" title="UTC">UTC</a>. Dates are the earliest possible dates and may change.</li>\n<li>Forward ports are at the front of the station according to its normal direction of travel and orientation (<a href="/wiki/Yaw,_pitch_and_roll#Aircraft_attitudes" class="mw-redirect" title="Yaw, pitch and roll">attitude</a>). Aft is at the rear of the station, used by spacecraft boosting the station\'s orbit. <a href="/wiki/Nadir" title="Nadir">Nadir</a> is closest the Earth, <a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">Zenith</a> is on top.</li>\n<li>Spacecraft operated by government agencies are indicated with \'Gov\' and under commercial arrangements are indicated with \'Com\'.</li>\n</ul>\n<dl>\n<dt>Key</dt>\n</dl>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg/35px-Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg/46px-Flag_of_Kazakhstan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="300" /></a></span> <a href="/wiki/Baikonur_(spaceport)" class="mw-redirect" title="Baikonur (spaceport)">Baikonur</a></td>\n<td nowrap="nowrap"><span class="flagicon"><a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia"><img alt="Russia" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f3/Flag_of_Russia.svg/45px-Flag_of_Russia.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></span></td>\n<td nowrap="nowrap">Gov</td>\n<td nowrap="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Roscosmos" title="Roscosmos">Roscosmos</a></td>\n<td style="background:wheat; white-space:nowrap;"><a href="/wiki/Uzlovoy_Module" title="Uzlovoy Module">Uzlovoy Module</a></td>\n<td>Progress M-UM</td>\n<td nowrap="nowrap">Module assembly</td>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Nauka_(ISS_module)" title="Nauka (ISS module)"><i>Nauka</i></a></td>\n<td nowrap="nowrap"><sup id="cite_ref-pietrobon_251-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pietrobon-251">[249]</a></sup></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Docking">Docking</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Docking_and_berthing_of_spacecraft" title="Docking and berthing of spacecraft">Docking and berthing of spacecraft</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Progress_M-14M.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Progress_M-14M.jpg/220px-Progress_M-14M.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Progress_M-14M.jpg/330px-Progress_M-14M.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Progress_M-14M.jpg/440px-Progress_M-14M.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3904" data-file-height="2600" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Progress_M-14M.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe <a href="/wiki/Progress_M-14M" title="Progress M-14M">Progress M-14M</a> resupply vehicle as it approaches the ISS in 2012. Over 50 unpiloted <a href="/wiki/Progress_(spacecraft)" title="Progress (spacecraft)">Progress</a> spacecraft have been sent with supplies during the lifetime of the station.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>All Russian spacecraft and self-propelled modules are able to rendezvous and dock to the space station without human intervention using the <a href="/wiki/Kurs_(docking_system)" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurs (docking system)">Kurs</a> docking system. Radar allows these vehicles to detect and intercept ISS from over 200 kilometres away. The European ATV uses star sensors and GPS to determine its intercept course. When it catches up it uses laser equipment to <a href="/wiki/Computer_vision" title="Computer vision">optically</a> recognise <i>Zvezda</i>, along with the Kurs system for redundancy. Crew supervise these craft, but do not intervene except to send abort commands in emergencies. The Japanese <a href="/wiki/H-II_Transfer_Vehicle" title="H-II Transfer Vehicle">H-II Transfer Vehicle</a> parks itself in progressively closer orbits to the station, and then awaits \'approach\' commands from the crew, until it is close enough for a robotic arm to grapple and berth the vehicle to the USOS. The American Space Shuttle was manually docked, and on missions with a <a href="/wiki/Multi-Purpose_Logistics_Module" title="Multi-Purpose Logistics Module">cargo container</a>, the container would be berthed to the Station with the use of manual robotic arms. Berthed craft can transfer <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Payload_Rack" title="International Standard Payload Rack">International Standard Payload Racks</a>. Japanese spacecraft berth for one to two months. Russian and European Supply craft can remain at the ISS for six months,<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[252]</a></sup> allowing great flexibility in crew time for loading and unloading of supplies and trash. NASA Shuttles could remain docked for 11\xe2\x80\x9312 days.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[253]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A side-on view of the ISS showing a Space Shuttle docked to the forward end, an ATV to the aft end and Soyuz &amp; Progress spacecraft projecting from the Russian segment." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/170px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/255px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg/340px-Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4032" data-file-height="6048" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Endeavour_docked_to_ISS.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\n<span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a></span> <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Endeavour" title="Space Shuttle Endeavour"><i>Endeavour</i></a>, <a href="/wiki/ATV-2" class="mw-redirect" title="ATV-2">ATV-2</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_TMA-21" title="Soyuz TMA-21">Soyuz TMA-21</a> and <a href="/wiki/Progress_M-10M" title="Progress M-10M">Progress M-10M</a> docked to the ISS, as seen from the departing <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_TMA-20" title="Soyuz TMA-20">Soyuz TMA-20</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>The American manual approach to docking allows greater initial flexibility and less complexity. The downside to this mode of operation is that each mission becomes unique and requires specialised training and planning, making the process more labour-intensive and expensive. The Russians pursued an automated methodology that used the crew in override or monitoring roles. Although the initial development costs were high, the system has become very reliable with standardisations that provide significant cost benefits in repetitive routine operations.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[254]</a></sup> An automated approach could allow assembly of modules orbiting other worlds prior to crew arrival.</p>\n<p>Soyuz spacecraft used for crew rotation also serve as lifeboats for emergency evacuation; they are replaced every six months and have been used once to remove excess crew after the <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle_Columbia_disaster" title="Space Shuttle Columbia disaster">Columbia disaster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[255]</a></sup> Expeditions require, on average, <span class="nowrap">2,722&#160;kg</span> of supplies, and as of 9 March 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, crews had consumed a total of around <span class="nowrap">22,000 meals</span>.<sup id="cite_ref-ISStD_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISStD-4">[4]</a></sup> Soyuz crew rotation flights and Progress resupply flights visit the station on average two and three times respectively each year,<sup id="cite_ref-Livelist_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Livelist-258">[256]</a></sup> with the ATV and HTV planned to visit annually from 2010 onwards.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> <a href="/wiki/Cygnus_spacecraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Cygnus spacecraft">Cygnus</a> and <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon">Dragon</a> were contracted to fly cargo to the station after retirement of the NASA Shuttle.<sup id="cite_ref-259" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-259">[257]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-260" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-260">[258]</a></sup></p>\n<p>From 26 February 2011 to 7 March 2011 four of the governmental partners (United States, ESA, Japan and Russia) had their spacecraft (NASA Shuttle, ATV, HTV, Progress and Soyuz) docked at the ISS, the only time this has happened to date.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">[259]</a></sup> On 25 May 2012, <a href="/wiki/SpaceX" title="SpaceX">SpaceX</a> became the world\'s first privately held company to send cargo, via the <a href="/wiki/SpaceX_Dragon" title="SpaceX Dragon">Dragon spacecraft</a>, to the International Space Station.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT-20120525_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-20120525-262">[260]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Launch_and_docking_windows">Launch and docking windows</span></h3>\n<p>Prior to a ship\'s docking to the ISS, navigation and attitude control (GNC) is handed over to the ground control of the ships\' country of origin. GNC is set to allow the station to drift in space, rather than fire its thrusters or turn using gyroscopes. The solar panels of the station are turned edge-on to the incoming ships, so residue from its thrusters does not damage the cells. When a NASA <a href="/wiki/Space_Shuttle" title="Space Shuttle">Space Shuttle</a> docked to the station, other ships were grounded, as the Shuttle\'s <a href="/wiki/Reinforced_carbon-carbon" class="mw-redirect" title="Reinforced carbon-carbon">reinforced carbon-carbon</a> wing leading edges, cameras, windows, and instruments were too much at risk from damage or contamination by thruster residue from other ships\' movements.</p>\n<p>Approximately 30% of NASA shuttle launch delays were caused by poor weather. Occasional priority was given to the Soyuz arrivals at the station where the Soyuz carried crew with time-critical cargoes such as biological experiment materials, also causing shuttle delays. Departure of the NASA shuttle was often delayed or prioritised according to weather over its two landing sites.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">[261]</a></sup> Whilst the Soyuz is capable of landing anywhere, anytime, its planned landing time and place is chosen to give consideration to helicopter pilots and ground recovery crew, to give acceptable flying weather and lighting conditions. Soyuz launches occur in adverse weather conditions, but the cosmodrome has been shut down on occasions when buried by snow drifts up to 6 metres in depth, hampering ground operations.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_aboard">Life aboard</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew_activities">Crew activities</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg/220px-Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg/330px-Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg/440px-Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2034" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Exp18home_nasa_big.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nCrewmember peers out of a window</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>A typical day for the crew begins with a wake-up at 06:00, followed by post-sleep activities and a morning inspection of the station. The crew then eats breakfast and takes part in a daily planning conference with Mission Control before starting work at around 08:10. The first scheduled exercise of the day follows, after which the crew continues work until 13:05. Following a one-hour lunch break, the afternoon consists of more exercise and work before the crew carries out its pre-sleep activities beginning at 19:30, including dinner and a crew conference. The scheduled sleep period begins at 21:30. In general, the crew works ten hours per day on a weekday, and five hours on Saturdays, with the rest of the time their own for relaxation or work catch-up.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">[262]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The time zone used aboard the ISS is <a href="/wiki/Coordinated_Universal_Time" title="Coordinated Universal Time">Coordinated Universal Time</a> (UTC). The windows are covered at night hours to give the impression of darkness because the station experiences 16 sunrises and sunsets per day. During visiting space shuttle missions, the ISS crew mostly follows the shuttle\'s <a href="/wiki/Mission_Elapsed_Time" title="Mission Elapsed Time">Mission Elapsed Time</a> (MET), which is a flexible time zone based on the launch time of the shuttle mission.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">[263]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">[264]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">[265]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The station provides crew quarters for each member of the expedition\'s crew, with two \'sleep stations\' in the <i>Zvezda</i> and four more installed in <i>Harmony</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">[266]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">[267]</a></sup> The American quarters are private, approximately person-sized soundproof booths. The Russian crew quarters include a small window, but provide less ventilation and sound proofing. A crew member can sleep in a crew quarter in a tethered sleeping bag, listen to music, use a laptop, and store personal items in a large drawer or in nets attached to the module\'s walls. The module also provides a reading lamp, a shelf and a desktop.<sup id="cite_ref-ESALife_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESALife-270">[268]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASACrewEquip_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-271">[269]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CSALife_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSALife-272">[270]</a></sup> Visiting crews have no allocated sleep module, and attach a sleeping bag to an available space on a wall. It is possible to sleep floating freely through the station, but this is generally avoided because of the possibility of bumping into sensitive equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-SRLife_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SRLife-273">[271]</a></sup> It is important that crew accommodations be well ventilated; otherwise, astronauts can wake up oxygen-deprived and gasping for air, because a bubble of their own exhaled carbon dioxide has formed around their heads.<sup id="cite_ref-ESALife_270-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESALife-270">[268]</a></sup></p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Christmas_on_the_International_Space_Station" title="Christmas on the International Space Station">Christmas on the International Space Station</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Meal_STS127.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Nine astronauts seated around a table covered in open cans of food strapped down to the table. In the background a selection of equipment is visible, as well as the salmon-coloured walls of the Unity node." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Meal_STS127.jpg/220px-Meal_STS127.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Meal_STS127.jpg/330px-Meal_STS127.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Meal_STS127.jpg/440px-Meal_STS127.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4288" data-file-height="2842" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Meal_STS127.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe crews of <a href="/wiki/STS-127" title="STS-127">STS-127</a> and <a href="/wiki/Expedition_20" title="Expedition 20">Expedition 20</a> enjoy a meal inside <i>Unity</i>.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Space_food" title="Space food">Space food</a></div>\n<p>Most of the food aboard is vacuum sealed in plastic bags. Cans are rare because they are heavy and expensive to transport. Preserved food is not highly regarded by the crew, and taste is reduced in microgravity.<sup id="cite_ref-ESALife_270-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESALife-270">[268]</a></sup> Therefore, effort is made to make the food more palatable, such as using more spices than in regular cooking. The crew looks forward to the arrival of any ships from Earth, as they bring fresh fruit and vegetables. Care is taken that foods do not create crumbs. Sauces are often used to avoid contaminating station equipment. Each crew member has individual food packages and cooks them using the on-board galley. The galley features two food warmers, a refrigerator added in November 2008, and a water dispenser that provides both heated and unheated water.<sup id="cite_ref-NASACrewEquip_271-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-271">[269]</a></sup> Drinks are provided as dehydrated powder that is mixed with water before consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-NASACrewEquip_271-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-271">[269]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-CSALife_272-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSALife-272">[270]</a></sup> Drinks and soups are sipped from plastic bags with straws. Solid food is eaten with a knife and fork attached to a tray with magnets to prevent them from floating away. Any food that floats away, including crumbs, must be collected to prevent it from clogging the station\'s air filters and other equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-CSALife_272-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSALife-272">[270]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hygiene">Hygiene</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zvezda_toilet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Zvezda_toilet.jpg/170px-Zvezda_toilet.jpg" width="170" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Zvezda_toilet.jpg/255px-Zvezda_toilet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/4/46/Zvezda_toilet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="263" data-file-height="400" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zvezda_toilet.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSpace toilet in the <i><a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)">Zvezda</a></i> service module</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Showers on space stations were introduced in the early 1970s on <i>Skylab</i> and <i>Salyut</i>&#160;3.<sup id="cite_ref-livingandworking_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-livingandworking-274">[272]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:139</sup> By <i>Salyut</i> 6, in the early 1980s, the crew complained of the complexity of showering in space, which was a monthly activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Portree1995-86_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Portree1995-86-275">[273]</a></sup> The ISS does not feature a shower; instead, crewmembers wash using a water jet and wet wipes, with soap dispensed from a toothpaste tube-like container. Crews are also provided with rinseless shampoo and edible toothpaste to save water.<sup id="cite_ref-SRLife_273-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SRLife-273">[271]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">[274]</a></sup></p>\n<p>There are two <a href="/wiki/Space_toilet" title="Space toilet">space toilets</a> on the ISS, both of Russian design, located in <i><a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)">Zvezda</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Tranquility_(ISS_module)" title="Tranquility (ISS module)">Tranquility</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NASACrewEquip_271-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-271">[269]</a></sup> These Waste and Hygiene Compartments use a fan-driven suction system similar to the Space Shuttle Waste Collection System. Astronauts first fasten themselves to the toilet seat, which is equipped with spring-loaded restraining bars to ensure a good seal.<sup id="cite_ref-ESALife_270-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESALife-270">[268]</a></sup> A lever operates a powerful fan and a suction hole slides open: the air stream carries the waste away. Solid waste is collected in individual bags which are stored in an aluminium container. Full containers are transferred to Progress spacecraft for disposal.<sup id="cite_ref-NASACrewEquip_271-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-271">[269]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">[275]</a></sup> Liquid waste is evacuated by a hose connected to the front of the toilet, with anatomically correct "urine funnel adapters" attached to the tube so that men and women can use the same toilet. <a href="/wiki/Urine_diversion" title="Urine diversion">The diverted urine</a> is collected and transferred to the Water Recovery System, where it is recycled into drinking water.<sup id="cite_ref-CSALife_272-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CSALife-272">[270]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Crew_health_and_safety">Crew health and safety</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Effect_of_spaceflight_on_the_human_body" title="Effect of spaceflight on the human body">Effect of spaceflight on the human body</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Radiation">Radiation</span></h3>\n<p>The ISS is partially protected from the space environment by Earth\'s <a href="/wiki/Magnetic_field" title="Magnetic field">magnetic field</a>. From an average distance of about 70,000&#160;km (43,000&#160;mi), depending on Solar activity, the <a href="/wiki/Magnetosphere" title="Magnetosphere">magnetosphere</a> begins to deflect <a href="/wiki/Solar_wind" title="Solar wind">solar wind</a> around Earth and ISS. <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">Solar flares</a> are still a hazard to the crew, who may receive only a few minutes warning. In 2005, during the initial \'proton storm\' of an X-3 class solar flare, the crew of <a href="/wiki/Expedition_10" title="Expedition 10">Expedition 10</a> took shelter in a more heavily shielded part of the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orbital_Segment" title="Russian Orbital Segment">ROS</a> designed for this purpose.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">[276]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">[277]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n<div id="mwe_player_2" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot;
