type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Low bandwidth WebM (160P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 160P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;160p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;240&quot; data-height=&quot;160&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;125424&quot; data-framerate=&quot;9&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv/Aurora_Australis.ogv.240p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;Small WebM (240P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 240P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;240p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;362&quot; data-height=&quot;240&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;247656&quot; data-framerate=&quot;9&quot;/&gt;&lt;source src=&quot;//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv/Aurora_Australis.ogv.360p.webm&quot; type=&quot;video/webm; codecs=&amp;quot;vp8, vorbis&amp;quot;&quot; data-title=&quot;WebM (360P)&quot; data-shorttitle=&quot;WebM 360P&quot; data-transcodekey=&quot;360p.webm&quot; data-width=&quot;542&quot; data-height=&quot;360&quot; data-bandwidth=&quot;500296&quot; data-framerate=&quot;9&quot;/&gt;&lt;/video&gt;&lt;/div&gt;"><img alt="File:Aurora Australis.ogv" style="width:220px;height:146px" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv/220px--Aurora_Australis.ogv.jpg" /><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f0/Aurora_Australis.ogv" title="Play media" target="new"><span class="play-btn-large"><span class="mw-tmh-playtext">Play media</span></span></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aurora_Australis.ogv" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nVideo of the <a href="/wiki/Aurora_Australis" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurora Australis">Aurora Australis</a> taken by the crew of <a href="/wiki/Expedition_28" title="Expedition 28">Expedition 28</a> on an ascending pass from south of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> to just north of Australia over the Indian Ocean.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Subatomic charged particles, primarily <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">protons</a> from <a href="/wiki/Cosmic_ray" title="Cosmic ray">cosmic rays</a> and solar wind, are normally absorbed by Earth\'s atmosphere. When they interact in sufficient quantity, their effect is visible to the naked eye in a phenomenon called an <a href="/wiki/Aurora" title="Aurora">aurora</a>. Outside Earth\'s atmosphere, crews are exposed to about 1 <a href="/wiki/Millisievert" class="mw-redirect" title="Millisievert">millisievert</a> each day, which is about a year of natural exposure on Earth. This results in a higher risk of cancer for astronauts. Radiation can penetrate living tissue and damage the <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chromosome" title="Chromosome">chromosomes</a> of <a href="/wiki/Lymphocytes" class="mw-redirect" title="Lymphocytes">lymphocytes</a>. These cells are central to the <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a>, and so any damage to them could contribute to the lower <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">immunity</a> experienced by astronauts. Radiation has also been linked to a higher incidence of <a href="/wiki/Cataract" title="Cataract">cataracts</a> in astronauts. Protective shielding and drugs may lower risks to an acceptable level.<sup id="cite_ref-JCB_47-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCB-47">[47]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Radiation levels on the ISS are about five times greater than those experienced by airline passengers and crew. Earth\'s electromagnetic field provides almost the same level of protection against solar and other radiation in low Earth orbit as in the stratosphere. For example, on a 12-hour flight an airline passenger would experience 0.1 millisieverts of radiation, or a rate of 0.2 millisieverts per day; only 1/5 the rate experienced by an astronaut in LEO. Additionally, airline passengers experience this level of radiation for a few hours of flight, while ISS crew are exposed for their whole stay.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">[278]</a></sup></p>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Coronal_mass_ejection" title="Coronal mass ejection">Coronal mass ejection</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aurora_(astronomy)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aurora (astronomy)">Aurora (astronomy)</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">Stress</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg/220px-Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg/330px-Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg/440px-Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="680" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nikolai_Budarin_in_a_sleep_station_in_Zvezda.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nCosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Nikolai_Budarin" title="Nikolai Budarin">Nikolai Budarin</a> at work inside <i><a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)">Zvezda</a></i> service module crew quarters</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>There is considerable evidence that <a href="/wiki/Psychosocial" title="Psychosocial">psychosocial</a> stressors are among the most important impediments to optimal crew morale and performance.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">[279]</a></sup> Cosmonaut <a href="/wiki/Valery_Ryumin" title="Valery Ryumin">Valery Ryumin</a> wrote in his journal during a particularly difficult period on board the <a href="/wiki/Salyut_6" title="Salyut 6"><i>Salyut</i> 6</a> space station: "All the conditions necessary for murder are met if you shut two men in a cabin measuring 18 feet by 20 and leave them together for two months."</p>\n<p>NASA\'s interest in <a href="/wiki/Stress_(psychological)" class="mw-redirect" title="Stress (psychological)">psychological stress</a> caused by space travel, initially studied when their manned missions began, was rekindled when astronauts joined cosmonauts on the Russian space station <i>Mir</i>. Common sources of stress in early American missions included maintaining high performance under public scrutiny and isolation from peers and family. The latter is still often a cause of stress on the ISS, such as when the mother of NASA Astronaut <a href="/wiki/Daniel_M._Tani" title="Daniel M. Tani">Daniel Tani</a> died in a car accident, and when Michael Fincke was forced to miss the birth of his second child.</p>\n<p>A study of the longest spaceflight concluded that the first three weeks are a critical period where attention is adversely affected because of the demand to adjust to the extreme change of environment.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">[280]</a></sup> <i>Skylab</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> three crews remained one, two, and three months respectively, long term crews on <i>Salyut</i>&#160;6, <a href="/wiki/Salyut_7" title="Salyut 7"><i>Salyut</i>&#160;7</a>, and the ISS last about five to six months and <i>Mir</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'s</span> expeditions often lasted longer. The ISS working environment includes further stress caused by living and working in cramped conditions with people from very different cultures who speak a different language. First-generation space stations had crews who spoke a single language; second and third-generation stations have crew from many cultures who speak many languages. The ISS is unique because visitors are not classed automatically into \'host\' or \'guest\' categories as with previous stations and spacecraft, and may not suffer from feelings of isolation in the same way. Crew members with a military pilot background and those with an academic science background or teachers and politicians may have problems understanding each other\'s jargon and worldview.</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medical">Medical</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Astronaut Frank De Winne is attached to the TVIS treadmill with bungee cords aboard the International Space Station" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg/220px-Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg/330px-Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg/440px-Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2864" data-file-height="2261" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Frank_De_Winne_on_treadmill_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nAstronaut <a href="/wiki/Frank_De_Winne" title="Frank De Winne">Frank De Winne</a> is attached to the <a href="/wiki/Treadmill_with_Vibration_Isolation_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Treadmill with Vibration Isolation System">TVIS treadmill</a> with bungee cords aboard the International Space Station</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Medical effects of long-term weightlessness include <a href="/wiki/Muscle_atrophy" title="Muscle atrophy">muscle atrophy</a>, deterioration of the skeleton <a href="/wiki/Spaceflight_osteopenia" title="Spaceflight osteopenia">(osteopenia)</a>, fluid redistribution, a slowing of the cardiovascular system, decreased production of red blood cells, balance disorders, and a weakening of the immune system. Lesser symptoms include loss of body mass, and puffiness of the face.<sup id="cite_ref-JCB_47-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JCB-47">[47]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Sleep is disturbed on the ISS regularly because of mission demands, such as incoming or departing ships. Sound levels in the station are unavoidably high; because the atmosphere is unable to <a href="/wiki/Thermosiphon" title="Thermosiphon">thermosiphon</a>, fans are required at all times to allow processing of the atmosphere which would stagnate in the freefall (zero-g) environment.</p>\n<p>To prevent some of these adverse <a href="/wiki/Physiological" class="mw-redirect" title="Physiological">physiological</a> effects, the station is equipped with two treadmills (including the <a href="/wiki/Treadmill_with_Vibration_Isolation_System" class="mw-redirect" title="Treadmill with Vibration Isolation System">COLBERT</a>), and the aRED (advanced Resistive Exercise Device) which enables various weightlifting exercises which add muscle but do not compensate for or raise astronauts\' reduced bone density,<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">[281]</a></sup> and a stationary bicycle; each astronaut spends at least two hours per day exercising on the equipment.<sup id="cite_ref-ESALife_270-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ESALife-270">[268]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NASACrewEquip_271-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NASACrewEquip-271">[269]</a></sup> Astronauts use bungee cords to strap themselves to the treadmill.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">[282]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285">[283]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Microbiological_environmental_hazards">Microbiological environmental hazards</span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Mir#Microbiological_environmental_hazards" title="Mir">Mir \xc2\xa7&#160;Microbiological environmental hazards</a></div>\n<p>Hazardous moulds which can foul air and water filters may develop aboard space stations. They can produce acids which degrade metal, glass, and rubber. They can also be harmful for the crew\'s health. Microbiological hazards have led to a development of the <a href="/wiki/LOCAD#Portable_Test_System" title="LOCAD">LOCAD-PTS</a> that can identify common bacteria and moulds faster than standard methods of <a href="/wiki/Cell_culture" title="Cell culture">culturing</a>, which may require a sample to be sent back to Earth.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">[284]</a></sup> As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=International_Space_Station&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, 76 types of unregulated micro-organisms have been detected on the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">[285]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Reduced humidity, paint with mould-killing chemicals, and antiseptic solutions can be used to prevent contamination in space stations. All materials used in the ISS are tested for resistance against fungi.<sup id="cite_ref-288" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-288">[286]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Threat_of_orbital_debris">Threat of orbital debris</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Space_debris" title="Space debris">Space debris</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:458px;max-width:458px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg/225px-SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg" width="225" height="178" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg/338px-SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg/450px-SDIO_KEW_Lexan_projectile.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3000" data-file-height="2375" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A 7&#160;g object (shown in centre) shot at 7&#160;km/s (23,000&#160;ft/s), the orbital velocity of the ISS, made this 15&#160;cm (5.9&#160;in) crater in a solid block of <a href="/wiki/Aluminium" title="Aluminium">aluminium</a>.</div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:227px;max-width:227px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Debris-GEO1280.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Debris-GEO1280.jpg/225px-Debris-GEO1280.jpg" width="225" height="180" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Debris-GEO1280.jpg/338px-Debris-GEO1280.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Debris-GEO1280.jpg/450px-Debris-GEO1280.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1024" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Radar" title="Radar">Radar</a>-trackable objects, including debris, with distinct ring of <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">geostationary</a> satellites</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>At the low altitudes at which the ISS orbits, there is a variety of space debris,<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289">[287]</a></sup> consisting of different objects including entire spent rocket stages, defunct satellites, explosion fragments\xe2\x80\x94including materials from <a href="/wiki/Anti-satellite_weapon" title="Anti-satellite weapon">anti-satellite weapon</a> tests, paint flakes, slag from solid rocket motors, and coolant released by <a href="/wiki/US-A" title="US-A">US-A</a> nuclear-powered satellites. These objects, in addition to natural <a href="/wiki/Micrometeoroid" title="Micrometeoroid">micrometeoroids</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">[288]</a></sup> are a significant threat. Large objects could destroy the station, but are less of a threat because their orbits can be predicted.<sup id="cite_ref-NSFdebris_291-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NSFdebris-291">[289]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">[290]</a></sup> Objects too small to be detected by optical and radar instruments, from approximately 1&#160;cm down to microscopic size, number in the trillions. Despite their small size, some of these objects are a threat because of their <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">kinetic energy</a> and direction in relation to the station. Spacesuits of spacewalking crew could puncture, causing <a href="/wiki/Space_exposure" title="Space exposure">exposure to vacuum</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-debrisdecomp_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debrisdecomp-293">[291]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Ballistic panels, also called micrometeorite shielding, are incorporated into the station to protect pressurised sections and critical systems. The type and thickness of these panels depends on their predicted exposure to damage. The station\'s shields and structure have different designs on the ROS and the USOS. On the USOS, a thin aluminium sheet is held apart from the hull and causes objects to shatter into a cloud before hitting the hull, thereby spreading the energy of impact. On the ROS, a carbon plastic honeycomb screen is spaced from the hull, an aluminium honeycomb screen is spaced from that, with a screen-vacuum thermal insulation covering, and glass cloth over the top. It is about 50% less likely to be punctured, and crew move to the ROS when the station is under threat. Punctures on the ROS would be contained within the panels which are 70&#160;cm square.</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_impact_risk.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/ISS_impact_risk.jpg/220px-ISS_impact_risk.jpg" width="220" height="174" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/ISS_impact_risk.jpg/330px-ISS_impact_risk.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/ca/ISS_impact_risk.jpg 2x" data-file-width="403" data-file-height="319" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_impact_risk.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nExample of <a href="/wiki/Risk_management" title="Risk management">risk management</a>: A NASA model showing areas at high risk from impact for the International Space Station.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Space debris is tracked remotely from the ground, and the station crew can be notified.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">[292]</a></sup> This allows for a <a href="/wiki/Debris_Avoidance_Manoeuvre" class="mw-redirect" title="Debris Avoidance Manoeuvre">Debris Avoidance Manoeuvre</a> (DAM) to be conducted, which uses thrusters on the Russian Orbital Segment to alter the station\'s orbital altitude, avoiding the debris. DAMs are not uncommon, taking place if computational models show the debris will approach within a certain threat distance. Eight DAMs had been performed prior to March 2009,<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">[293]</a></sup> the first seven between October 1999 and May 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-ODOct08_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODOct08-296">[294]</a></sup> Usually, the orbit is raised by one or two kilometres by means of an increase in orbital velocity of the order of 1&#160;m/s. Unusually, there was a lowering of 1.7&#160;km on 27 August 2008, the first such lowering for 8 years.<sup id="cite_ref-ODOct08_296-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ODOct08-296">[294]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">[295]</a></sup> There were two DAMs in 2009, on 22 March and 17 July.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">[296]</a></sup> If a threat from orbital debris is identified too late for a DAM to be safely conducted, the station crew close all the hatches aboard the station and retreat into their <a href="/wiki/Soyuz_spacecraft" class="mw-redirect" title="Soyuz spacecraft">Soyuz spacecraft</a>, so that they would be able to evacuate in the event the station was seriously damaged by the debris. This partial station evacuation has occurred on 13 March 2009, 28 June 2011, 24 March 2012 and 16 June 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">[297]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">[298]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="End_of_mission">End of mission</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg/220px-Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg/330px-Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg/440px-Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3773" data-file-height="2434" /></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jules_Verne_Automated_Transfer_Vehicle_re-enters_Earth%27s_atmosphere.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMany ISS resupply spacecraft have already undergone <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">atmospheric re-entry</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Jules_Verne_ATV" title="Jules Verne ATV">Jules Verne ATV</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>According to a 2009 report, <a href="/wiki/S.P._Korolev_Rocket_and_Space_Corporation_Energia" class="mw-redirect" title="S.P. Korolev Rocket and Space Corporation Energia">Space Corporation Energia</a> is considering methods to remove from the station some modules of the Russian Orbital Segment when the end of mission is reached and use them as a basis for a new station, called the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_Piloted_Assembly_and_Experiment_Complex" title="Orbital Piloted Assembly and Experiment Complex">Orbital Piloted Assembly and Experiment Complex</a> (OPSEK). The modules under consideration for removal from the current ISS include the <a href="/wiki/Multipurpose_Laboratory_Module" class="mw-redirect" title="Multipurpose Laboratory Module">Multipurpose Laboratory Module</a> (Nauka), currently scheduled to be launched in 2017, and other Russian modules which are planned to be attached to Nauka afterwards. Those modules would be within their useful lives in 2016 or 2020. The report presents a statement from an unnamed Russian engineer that, based on the experience from <i>Mir</i>, a 30-year life should be possible, except for micrometeorite damage, because the Russian modules have been built with on-orbit refurbishment in mind.<sup id="cite_ref-RussiaSave_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RussiaSave-301">[299]</a></sup></p>\n<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/Outer_Space_Treaty" title="Outer Space Treaty">Outer Space Treaty</a> the United States and Russia are legally responsible for all modules they have launched.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">[300]</a></sup> In ISS planning, NASA examined options including returning the station to Earth via shuttle missions (deemed too expensive, as the USOS is not designed for disassembly and this would require at least 27 shuttle missions<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">[301]</a></sup>), natural orbital decay with random reentry similar to <i><a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a></i>, boosting the station to a higher altitude (which would delay reentry) and a controlled targeted de-orbit to a remote ocean area.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSEIS_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSEIS-304">[302]</a></sup></p>\n<p>A controlled deorbit into a remote ocean was found to be technically feasible only with Russia\'s assistance.<sup id="cite_ref-ISSEIS_304-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISSEIS-304">[302]</a></sup> The Russian Space Agency has experience from de-orbiting the <a href="/wiki/Salyut_4" title="Salyut 4"><i>Salyut</i> 4</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salyut_5" title="Salyut 5">5</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salyut_6" title="Salyut 6">6</a>, <a href="/wiki/Salyut_7" title="Salyut 7">7</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/Mir" title="Mir">Mir</a></i> space stations; NASA\'s first intentional controlled de-orbit of a satellite (the <a href="/wiki/Compton_Gamma_Ray_Observatory" title="Compton Gamma Ray Observatory">Compton Gamma Ray Observatory</a>) occurred in 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">[303]</a></sup> As of late 2010, the preferred plan is to use a slightly modified Progress spacecraft to de-orbit the ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-deo_306-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deo-306">[304]</a></sup> This plan was seen as the simplest, cheapest and with the highest margin.<sup id="cite_ref-deo_306-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deo-306">[304]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Skylab" title="Skylab">Skylab</a></i>, the only space station built and launched entirely by the US, decayed from orbit slowly over 5 years, and no attempt was made to de-orbit it using a <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_entry" title="Atmospheric entry">deorbital burn</a>. Remains of <i>Skylab</i> hit populated areas of <a href="/wiki/Esperance,_Western_Australia" title="Esperance, Western Australia">Esperance, Western Australia</a><sup id="cite_ref-debris_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-debris-307">[305]</a></sup> without injuries or loss of life.</p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Exploration_Gateway_Platform" title="Exploration Gateway Platform">Exploration Gateway Platform</a>, a discussion by NASA and Boeing at the end of 2011, suggested using leftover USOS hardware and \'<a href="/wiki/Zvezda_(ISS_module)" title="Zvezda (ISS module)"><i>Zvezda</i></a> 2\'  [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] as a refuelling depot and service station located at one of the Earth-Moon <a href="/wiki/Lagrange_points" class="mw-redirect" title="Lagrange points">Lagrange points</a>, L1 or L2. The entire USOS cannot be reused and will be discarded, but some Russian modules are planned to be reused. <i><a href="/wiki/Nauka_(ISS_module)" title="Nauka (ISS module)">Nauka</a></i>, the <a href="/wiki/Uzlovoy_Module" title="Uzlovoy Module">Uzlovoy Module</a>, two science power platforms and <i>Rassvet</i>, launched between 2010 and 2015 and joined to the ROS, may be separated to form <a href="/wiki/OPSEK" class="mw-redirect" title="OPSEK">OPSEK</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">[306]</a></sup> <i>Nauka</i> will be used in the station, whose main goal is supporting manned deep space exploration. OPSEK will orbit at a higher inclination of 71 degrees, allowing observation to and from all of the Russian Federation.</p>\n<p>In February 2015, Roscosmos announced that it would remain a part of the ISS programme until 2024.<sup id="cite_ref-sn20150225_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sn20150225-23">[23]</a></sup> Nine months earlier\xe2\x80\x94in response to US sanctions against Russia over the <a href="/wiki/2014_Crimean_crisis" class="mw-redirect" title="2014 Crimean crisis">conflict in the Crimea</a>\xe2\x80\x94Russian Deputy Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Rogozin" title="Dmitry Rogozin">Dmitry Rogozin</a> had stated that Russia would reject a US request to prolong the orbiting station\'s use beyond 2020, and would only supply rocket engines to the US for non-military satellite launches.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">[307]</a></sup></p>\n<p>A proposed modification that would reuse some of the ISS American and European segments is to attach a <a href="/wiki/VASIMR" class="mw-redirect" title="VASIMR">VASIMR</a> drive module to the vacated Node with its own onboard power source. This would allow long-term reliability testing of the concept for less cost than building a dedicated space station from scratch.<sup id="cite_ref-nbcnews_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nbcnews-310">[308]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On 28 March 2015, Russian sources announced that Roscosmos and NASA had agreed to collaborate on the development of a replacement for the current ISS.<sup id="cite_ref-independent20150328_25-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-independent20150328-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rtcom20150328_26-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rtcom20150328-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/w/index.php?title=Igor_Komarov&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Igor Komarov (page does not exist)">Igor Komarov</a>, the head of Russia\'s Roscosmos, made the announcement with NASA administrator Charles Bolden at his side. Komarov said "Roscosmos together with NASA will work on the programme of a future orbital station", "We agreed that the group of countries taking part in the ISS project will work on the future project of a new orbital station", "The first step is that the ISS will operate until 2024", and that Roscosmos and NASA "do not rule out that the station\'s flight could be extended".<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">[309]</a></sup> In a statement provided to SpaceNews on 28 March, NASA spokesman David Weaver said the agency appreciated the Russian commitment to extending the ISS, but did not confirm any plans for a future space station.<sup id="cite_ref-no_plans_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-no_plans-28">[28]</a></sup></p>\n<p>On 30 September 2015, Boeing\'s contract with NASA as prime contractor for the ISS was extended to 30 September 2020. Part of Boeing\'s services under the contract will relate to extending the station\'s primary structural hardware past 2020 to the end of 2028.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">[310]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Regarding extending the ISS, on 15 November 2016 General Director Vladimir Solntsev of RSC Energia stated "Maybe the ISS will receive continued resources. Today we discussed the possibility of using the station until 2028," and "Much will depend on the political moments in relations with the Americans, with the new administration. It will be discussed."<sup id="cite_ref-sputnik20161115_313-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sputnik20161115-313">[311]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-sputnik20161116_314-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sputnik20161116-314">[312]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost">Cost</span></h2>\n<p>The ISS has been described as the most expensive single item ever constructed.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">[313]</a></sup> In 2010 the cost was expected to be $150&#160;billion. This includes NASA\'s budget of $58.7&#160;billion (inflation-unadjusted) for the station from 1985 to 2015 ($72.4&#160;billion in 2010 dollars), Russia\'s $12&#160;billion, Europe\'s $5&#160;billion, Japan\'s $5&#160;billion, Canada\'s $2&#160;billion, and the cost of 36 shuttle flights to build the station; estimated at $1.4&#160;billion each, or $50.4&#160;billion in total. Assuming 20,000 person-days of use from 2000 to 2015 by two- to six-person crews, each person-day would cost $7.5&#160;million, less than half the inflation-adjusted $19.6&#160;million ($5.5&#160;million before inflation) per person-day of <i>Skylab</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-lafleur20100308_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lafleur20100308-316">[314]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span title="Brazil">Brazil</a></b></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sightings_from_Earth">Sightings from Earth</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:461px;max-width:461px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:225px;max-width:225px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A fuzzy image of the ISS set against a black background, with a smaller, cylindrical object visible to the left of the station." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg/223px-Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg" width="223" height="153" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg/335px-Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/23/Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg/446px-Isshtv120090917200858nm.jpg 2x" data-file-width="744" data-file-height="512" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">The ISS and HTV photographed using a telescope-mounted camera by <a href="/wiki/Ralf_Vandebergh" title="Ralf Vandebergh">Ralf Vandebergh</a></div>\n</div>\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:232px;max-width:232px">\n<div class="thumbimage"><a href="/wiki/File:ISS_2008-01-10.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A view of a dark blue, starry sky with a white line visible from the bottom-left to the top-right of the image. A tree is visible to the bottom right." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ISS_2008-01-10.jpg/230px-ISS_2008-01-10.jpg" width="230" height="153" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ISS_2008-01-10.jpg/345px-ISS_2008-01-10.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ISS_2008-01-10.jpg/460px-ISS_2008-01-10.jpg 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" /></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">A time exposure of a station pass</div>\n</div>\n<div style="clear:left"></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naked_eye">Naked eye</span></h3>\n<p>The ISS is visible to the <a href="/wiki/Naked_eye" title="Naked eye">naked eye</a> as a slow-moving, bright white dot because of reflected sunlight, and can be seen in the hours after sunset and before sunrise, when the station remains sunlit but the ground and sky are dark.<sup id="cite_ref-Price2005_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Price2005-317">[315]</a></sup> The ISS takes about 10 minutes to pass from one horizon to another, and will only be visible part of that time because of moving into or out of the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_shadow" title="Earth\'s shadow">Earth\'s shadow</a>. Because of the size of its reflective surface area, the ISS is the brightest artificial object in the sky, excluding <a href="/wiki/Satellite_flare" title="Satellite flare">flares</a>, with an approximate maximum <a href="/wiki/Apparent_magnitude" title="Apparent magnitude">magnitude</a> of \xe2\x88\x924 when overhead (similar to Venus). The ISS, like many satellites including the <a href="/wiki/Iridium_constellation" class="mw-redirect" title="Iridium constellation">Iridium constellation</a>, can also produce flares of up to 8 or 16 times the brightness of <a href="/wiki/Venus_(planet)" class="mw-redirect" title="Venus (planet)">Venus</a> as sunlight glints off reflective surfaces.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">[316]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-haydenplanetarium_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haydenplanetarium-319">[317]</a></sup> The ISS is also visible during broad daylight conditions, albeit with a great deal more effort.</p>\n<p>Tools are provided by a number of websites such as <a href="/wiki/Heavens-Above" title="Heavens-Above">Heavens-Above</a> (see <a href="#Live_viewing"><i>Live viewing</i></a> below) as well as <a href="/wiki/Smartphone" title="Smartphone">smartphone</a> applications that use the known <a href="/wiki/Ephemeris" title="Ephemeris">orbital data</a> and the observer\'s longitude and latitude to predict when the ISS will be visible (weather permitting), where the station will appear to rise to the observer, the altitude above the horizon it will reach and the duration of the pass before the station disappears to the observer either by setting below the horizon or entering into Earth\'s shadow.<sup id="cite_ref-see_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-see-320">[318]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">[319]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">[320]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-daytime_visibility_323-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-daytime_visibility-323">[321]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In November 2012 NASA launched its "Spot the Station" service, which sends people text and email alerts when the station is due to fly above their town.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">[322]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The station is visible from 95% of the inhabited land on Earth, but is not visible from extreme northern or southern latitudes.<sup id="cite_ref-MCC_Answer_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MCC_Answer-208">[206]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Astrophotography">Astrophotography</span></h3>\n<p>Using a telescope-mounted camera to photograph the station is a popular hobby for astronomers,<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">[323]</a></sup> whilst using a mounted camera to photograph the Earth and stars is a popular hobby for crew.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">[324]</a></sup> The use of a telescope or binoculars allows viewing of the ISS during daylight hours.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">[325]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Parisian engineer and astrophotographer Thierry Legault, known for his photos of spaceships crossing the Sun (called <a href="/wiki/Occultation" title="Occultation">occultation</a>), travelled to Oman in 2011, to photograph the Sun, Moon and space station all lined up.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">[326]</a></sup> Legault, who received the Marius Jacquemetton award from the <a href="/wiki/Soci%C3%A9t%C3%A9_astronomique_de_France" title="Soci\xc3\xa9t\xc3\xa9 astronomique de France">Soci\xc3\xa9t\xc3\xa9 astronomique de France</a> in 1999, and other hobbyists, use websites that predict when the ISS will pass in front of the Sun or Moon and from what location those passes will be visible.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<table role="presentation" class="metadata mbox-small" title="Space architecture">Space architecture</a></li>\n</ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-200"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-200">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Privately funded travellers who have objected to the term include Dennis Tito, the first such traveller (<i>Associated Press</i>, 8 May 2001), <a href="/wiki/Mark_Shuttleworth" title="Mark Shuttleworth">Mark Shuttleworth</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Ubuntu_(operating_system)" title="Ubuntu (operating system)">Ubuntu</a> (<i>Associated Press, The Spokesman Review, 6 January 2002, p. A4</i>), Gregory Olsen and <a href="/wiki/Richard_Garriott" title="Richard Garriott">Richard Garriott</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Canadian astronaut Bob Thirsk said the term does not seem appropriate, referring to his crewmate, <a href="/wiki/Guy_Lalibert%C3%A9" title="Guy Lalibert\xc3\xa9">Guy Lalibert\xc3\xa9</a>, founder of <a href="/wiki/Cirque_du_Soleil" title="Cirque du Soleil">Cirque du Soleil</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> Anousheh Ansari denied being a tourist<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> and took offence at the term.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup></span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-202"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-202">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">ESA director J\xc3\xb6rg Feustel-B\xc3\xbcechl said in 2001 that Russia had no right to send \'amateurs\' to the ISS. A \'stand-off\' occurred at the Johnson Space Centre between Commander Talgat Musabayev and NASA manager Robert Cabana. Cabana refused to train Dennis Tito, a member of Musabayev\'s crew along with Yuri Baturin. The commander argued that Tito had trained 700 hours in the last year and was as qualified as any NASA astronaut, and refused to allow his crew to be trained on the American portions of the station without Tito. Cabana stated training could not begin, and the commander returned with his crew to their hotel.</span></li>\n</ol>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-ISS_stats-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-ISS_stats_1-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-ISS_stats_1-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Garcia, Mark (1 October 2015). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.nasa.gov/mission_pages/station/main/onthestation/facts_and_figures.html">"About the Space Station: Facts and Figures"</a>.
